\
**George Washington** (February 22, 1732\'a0\'96 December 14, 1799) was an American military officer, statesman, and [Founding Father](/wiki/Founding_Fathers_of_the_United_States "Founding Fathers of the United States") who served as the first [president of the United States](/wiki/President_of_the_United_States "President of the United States") from 1789 to 1797. Appointed by the [Second Continental Congress](/wiki/Second_Continental_Congress "Second Continental Congress") as commander of the [Continental Army](/wiki/Continental_Army "Continental Army") in June 1775, Washington led [Patriot](/wiki/Patriot_(American_Revolution) "Patriot (American Revolution)") forces to victory in the [American Revolutionary War](/wiki/American_Revolutionary_War "American Revolutionary War") and then served as president of the [Constitutional Convention](/wiki/Constitutional_Convention_(United_States) "Constitutional Convention (United States)") in 1787, which drafted and ratified the [Constitution of the United States](/wiki/Constitution_of_the_United_States "Constitution of the United States") and established the [American federal government](/wiki/Federal_government_of_the_United_States "Federal government of the United States"). Washington has thus been called the "[Father of his Country](/wiki/Father_of_the_Nation "Father of the Nation")".\
\
Washington's first public office, from 1749 to 1750, was as [surveyor](/wiki/Surveying "Surveying") of [Culpeper County](/wiki/Culpeper_County,_Virginia "Culpeper County, Virginia") in the [Colony of Virginia](/wiki/Colony_of_Virginia "Colony of Virginia"). He subsequently received military training and was assigned command of the [Virginia Regiment](/wiki/Virginia_Regiment "Virginia Regiment") during the [French and Indian War](/wiki/French_and_Indian_War "French and Indian War"). He was later elected to the [Virginia House of Burgesses](/wiki/House_of_Burgesses "House of Burgesses") and was named a delegate to the Continental Congress in [Philadelphia](/wiki/Philadelphia "Philadelphia"), which appointed him [Commander-in-Chief](/wiki/Commanding_General_of_the_United_States_Army "Commanding General of the United States Army") of the Continental Army. Washington led American forces to a decisive victory over the [British](/wiki/British_Army_during_the_American_Revolutionary_War "British Army during the American Revolutionary War") in the Revolutionary War, leading the British to sign the [Treaty of Paris](/wiki/Treaty_of_Paris_(1783) "Treaty of Paris (1783)"), which acknowledged the sovereignty and independence of the [United States](/wiki/United_States "United States"). He [resigned his commission](/wiki/George_Washington%27s_resignation_as_commander-in-chief "George Washington's resignation as commander-in-chief") in 1783 after the conclusion of the Revolutionary War.\
\
Washington played an indispensable role in adopting and ratifying the Constitution, which replaced the [Articles of Confederation](/wiki/Articles_of_Confederation "Articles of Confederation") in 1789. He was then twice elected president by the [Electoral College](/wiki/United_States_Electoral_College "United States Electoral College") unanimously. As the first U.S. president, Washington implemented a strong, well-financed national government while remaining impartial in a fierce rivalry that emerged between cabinet members [Thomas Jefferson](/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson "Thomas Jefferson") and [Alexander Hamilton](/wiki/Alexander_Hamilton "Alexander Hamilton"). During the [French Revolution](/wiki/French_Revolution "French Revolution"), he proclaimed a [policy of neutrality](/wiki/Proclamation_of_Neutrality "Proclamation of Neutrality") while sanctioning the [Jay Treaty](/wiki/Jay_Treaty "Jay Treaty"). He set enduring precedents for the [office of president](/wiki/Executive_Office_of_the_President_of_the_United_States "Executive Office of the President of the United States"), including use of the title "[Mr. President](/wiki/President_(government_title)#United_States "President (government title)")" and the [two-term tradition](/wiki/Twenty-second_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution#Background "Twenty-second Amendment to the United States Constitution"). His 1796 [farewell address](/wiki/George_Washington%27s_Farewell_Address "George Washington's Farewell Address") became a preeminent statement on [republicanism](/wiki/Republicanism_in_the_United_States "Republicanism in the United States") in which he wrote about the importance of national unity and the dangers regionalism, partisanship, and foreign influence pose to it.\
\
Washington has been [memorialized](/wiki/List_of_memorials_to_George_Washington "List of memorials to George Washington") by monuments, a [federal holiday](/wiki/Presidents%27_Day "Presidents' Day"), [various media depictions](/wiki/Cultural_depictions_of_George_Washington "Cultural depictions of George Washington"), geographical locations including [the national capital](/wiki/Washington,_D.C. "Washington, D.C."), the [State of Washington](/wiki/Washington_(state) "Washington (state)"), [stamps](/wiki/Presidents_of_the_United_States_on_U.S._postage_stamps#George_Washington "Presidents of the United States on U.S. postage stamps"), and [currency](/wiki/United_States_dollar "United States dollar"). He is ranked among [the greatest U.S. presidents](/wiki/Historical_rankings_of_presidents_of_the_United_States "Historical rankings of presidents of the United States"). In 1976, Washington was posthumously promoted to the rank of [General of the Armies](/wiki/General_of_the_Armies "General of the Armies"), the highest rank in the [U.S. Army](/wiki/United_States_Army "United States Army"). His legacy has become increasingly controversial over time, however, as a result of his [ownership of slaves and his relationship](/wiki/George_Washington_and_slavery "George Washington and slavery") with [slavery](/wiki/Slavery_in_the_United_States "Slavery in the United States"). His historical reputation has also been complicated in modern discourse by his policy of [assimilating Native Americans](/wiki/Cultural_assimilation_of_Native_Americans "Cultural assimilation of Native Americans") into [Anglo-American](/wiki/Anglo-Americans "Anglo-Americans") culture and waging war against indigenous peoples during the [Revolutionary War](/wiki/American_Revolutionary_War "American Revolutionary War") and the [Northwest Indian War](/wiki/Northwest_Indian_War "Northwest Indian War").\
\
Early life (1732\'961752)\
----------------------\
\
Further information: [Washington family](/wiki/Washington_family "Washington family") and [British America](/wiki/British_America "British America")\
\
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Residence_of_the_Washington_Family_on_the_Rappahannock.jpg/220px-Residence_of_the_Washington_Family_on_the_Rappahannock.jpg)](/wiki/File:Residence_of_the_Washington_Family_on_the_Rappahannock.jpg)\
\
[Ferry Farm](/wiki/Ferry_Farm "Ferry Farm"), the [Washington family](/wiki/Washington_family "Washington family")'s residence on the [Rappahannock River](/wiki/Rappahannock_River "Rappahannock River") in [Stafford County, Virginia](/wiki/Stafford_County,_Virginia "Stafford County, Virginia"), where Washington spent much of his youth\
\
George Washington was born on February 22, 1732,[\\[a\\]](#cite_note-3) at [Popes Creek](/wiki/George_Washington_Birthplace_National_Monument "George Washington Birthplace National Monument") in [Westmoreland County](/wiki/Westmoreland_County,_Virginia "Westmoreland County, Virginia"), Virginia.[\\[3\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow20103\'966-4) He was the first of six children of [Augustine](/wiki/Augustine_Washington "Augustine Washington") and [Mary Ball Washington](/wiki/Mary_Ball_Washington "Mary Ball Washington").[\\[4\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling20023Chernow20105\'967-5) His father was a [justice of the peace](/wiki/Justice_of_the_peace "Justice of the peace") and a prominent public figure who had four additional children from his first marriage to Jane Butler.[\\[5\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling20099Chernow20106\'968-6) The family moved to [Little Hunting Creek](/wiki/Little_Hunting_Creek "Little Hunting Creek") in 1734 before eventually settling in [Ferry Farm](/wiki/Ferry_Farm "Ferry Farm") near [Fredericksburg, Virginia](/wiki/Fredericksburg,_Virginia "Fredericksburg, Virginia"). When Augustine died in 1743, Washington inherited Ferry Farm and ten slaves; his older half-brother [Lawrence](/wiki/Lawrence_Washington_(1718%E2%80%931752) "Lawrence Washington (1718\'961752)") inherited Little Hunting Creek and renamed it [Mount Vernon](/wiki/Mount_Vernon "Mount Vernon").[\\[6\\]](#cite_note-Slavery-at-Mount-Vernon-7)[\\[7\\]](#cite_note-8)\
\
Washington did not have the formal education his elder brothers received at [Appleby Grammar School](/wiki/Appleby_Grammar_School "Appleby Grammar School") in England, but he did attend the [Lower Church](/wiki/Lower_Church "Lower Church") School in [Hartfield](/wiki/Hartfield,_Virginia "Hartfield, Virginia"). He learned mathematics, [trigonometry](/wiki/Trigonometry "Trigonometry"), and land [surveying](/wiki/Surveying "Surveying"), and became a talented draftsman and [mapmaker](/wiki/Cartography "Cartography"). By early adulthood, he was writing with "considerable force" and "precision".[\\[8\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow201010\'9612Ferling200214Ferling19885\'966-9) As a teenager, to practice his penmanship, Washington compiled over a hundred rules for social interaction styled _Rules of Civility and Decent Behaviour in Company and Conversation_, copied from an English translation of a French book of manners.[\\[9\\]](#cite_note-10)\
\
Washington often visited Mount Vernon and [Belvoir](/wiki/Belvoir_(plantation) "Belvoir (plantation)"), the plantation of [William Fairfax](/wiki/William_Fairfax "William Fairfax"), Lawrence's father-in-law. Fairfax became Washington's patron and surrogate father, and Washington spent a month in 1748 with a team surveying Fairfax's [Shenandoah Valley](/wiki/Shenandoah_Valley "Shenandoah Valley") property.[\\[10\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow201010,_19Ferling200214\'9615Randall199736-11) The following year, he received a surveyor's license from the [College of William & Mary](/wiki/College_of_William_%26_Mary "College of William & Mary").[\\[b\\]](#cite_note-13) Even though Washington had not served the customary apprenticeship, Fairfax appointed him surveyor of [Culpeper County, Virginia](/wiki/Culpeper_County,_Virginia "Culpeper County, Virginia"), where he took his oath of office July 20, 1749. He subsequently familiarized himself with the frontier region, and though he resigned from the job in 1750, he continued to do surveys west of the [Blue Ridge Mountains](/wiki/Blue_Ridge_Mountains "Blue Ridge Mountains").[\\[11\\]](#cite_note-professional-surveys-12) By 1752, he had bought almost 1,500 acres (600\'a0ha) in the Valley and owned 2,315 acres (937\'a0ha).[\\[12\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFitzpatrick1936v._19,_p._510Chernow201022\'9623-14)\
\
In 1751, Washington made his only trip abroad when he accompanied Lawrence to [Barbados](/wiki/Barbados "Barbados"), hoping the climate would cure his brother's tuberculosis.[\\[13\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow201024-15) Washington contracted [smallpox](/wiki/Smallpox "Smallpox") during that trip, which left his face slightly scarred.[\\[14\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlexner19748Chernow201025-16) Lawrence died in 1752, and Washington leased Mount Vernon from his widow Anne; he inherited it outright after her death in 1761.[\\[15\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow201026,_98-17)\
\
Colonial military career (1752\'961758)\
------------------------------------\
\
Lawrence Washington's service as adjutant general of the [Virginia militia](/wiki/Virginia_militia "Virginia militia") inspired George to seek a commission. Virginia's lieutenant governor, [Robert Dinwiddie](/wiki/Robert_Dinwiddie "Robert Dinwiddie"), appointed Washington as a major and commander of one of the four militia districts. The British and French were competing for control of the [Ohio Valley](/wiki/Ohio_River "Ohio River"): the British were constructing forts along the Ohio River, and the French between the [Ohio River](/wiki/Ohio_River "Ohio River") and [Lake Erie](/wiki/Lake_Erie "Lake Erie").[\\[16\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnderson200731\'9632Chernow201026\'9627,_31-18)\
\
In October 1753, Dinwiddie appointed Washington as a special envoy. He had sent Washington to demand French forces to vacate land that was claimed by the British. Washington was also appointed to make peace with the [Iroquois Confederacy](/wiki/Iroquois_Confederacy "Iroquois Confederacy"), and to gather further intelligence about the French forces.[\\[17\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling200915\'9616-19) Washington met with Half-King [Tanacharison](/wiki/Tanacharison "Tanacharison"), and other Iroquois chiefs, at [Logstown](/wiki/Logstown "Logstown"), and gathered information about the numbers and locations of the French forts, as well as intelligence concerning individuals taken prisoner by the French. Washington was nicknamed [Conotocaurius](/wiki/Town_Destroyer "Town Destroyer") by Tanacharison. The name, meaning "devourer of villages", had been given to his great-grandfather [John Washington](/wiki/John_Washington "John Washington") in the late 17th century by the [Susquehannock](/wiki/Susquehannock "Susquehannock").[\\[18\\]](#cite_note-20)\
\
Washington's party reached the Ohio River in November 1753, and was intercepted by a French patrol. The party was escorted to [Fort Le Boeuf](/wiki/Fort_Le_Boeuf "Fort Le Boeuf"), where Washington was received in a friendly manner. He delivered the British demand to vacate to the French commander [Saint-Pierre](/wiki/Jacques_Legardeur_de_Saint-Pierre "Jacques Legardeur de Saint-Pierre"), but the French refused to leave. Saint-Pierre gave Washington his official answer after a few days' delay, as well as food and winter clothing for his party's journey back to Virginia.[\\[19\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling200915\'9618Lengel200523\'9624Randall199774Chernow201026\'9627,_31-21) Washington completed the precarious mission in 77 days, in difficult winter conditions, achieving a measure of distinction when his report was published in Virginia and London.[\\[20\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFitzpatrick193619,_pp._510\'96511Ferling200915\'9618-22)\
\
### French and Indian War\
\
Main article: [George Washington in the French and Indian War](/wiki/George_Washington_in_the_French_and_Indian_War "George Washington in the French and Indian War")\
\
Further information: [French and Indian War](/wiki/French_and_Indian_War "French and Indian War") and [Seven Years' War](/wiki/Seven_Years%27_War "Seven Years' War")\
\
[![Night scene depicting Washington at center, standing among officers and Indians, around a lamp, holding a war council](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/The_Night_Council_At_Fort_Necessity_from_the_Darlington_Collection_of_Engraving-_detail.jpg/260px-The_Night_Council_At_Fort_Necessity_from_the_Darlington_Collection_of_Engraving-_detail.jpg)](/wiki/File:The_Night_Council_At_Fort_Necessity_from_the_Darlington_Collection_of_Engraving-_detail.jpg)\
\
Lieutenant Colonel Washington holds night council during the [Battle of Fort Necessity](/wiki/Battle_of_Fort_Necessity "Battle of Fort Necessity") in [Fayette County, Pennsylvania](/wiki/Fayette_County,_Pennsylvania "Fayette County, Pennsylvania")\
\
In February 1754, Dinwiddie promoted Washington to lieutenant colonel and second-in-command of the 300-strong [Virginia Regiment](/wiki/Virginia_Regiment "Virginia Regiment"), with orders to confront French forces at the [Forks of the Ohio](/wiki/Point_State_Park "Point State Park").[\\[21\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow201031\'9632Ferling200918\'9619-23) Washington set out with half the regiment in April and soon learned a French force of 1,000 had begun construction of [Fort Duquesne](/wiki/Fort_Duquesne "Fort Duquesne") there. In May, having set up a [defensive position](/wiki/Fort_Necessity_National_Battlefield "Fort Necessity National Battlefield") at Great Meadows, he learned that the French had made camp seven miles (11\'a0km) away; he decided to take the offensive.[\\[22\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow201041\'9642-24)\
\
The French detachment proved to be only about 50 men, so Washington advanced on May 28 with a small force of Virginians and Indian allies to ambush them.[\\[c\\]](#cite_note-26)[\\[24\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow201042-27) During the [ambush](/wiki/Battle_of_Jumonville_Glen "Battle of Jumonville Glen"), French forces were killed outright with muskets and hatchets, including French commander [Joseph Coulon de Jumonville](/wiki/Joseph_Coulon_de_Jumonville "Joseph Coulon de Jumonville"), who had been carrying a diplomatic message for the British. The French later found their countrymen dead and scalped, blaming Washington, who had retreated to Fort Necessity.[\\[25\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling200924\'9625-28)\
\
The full Virginia Regiment joined Washington at Fort Necessity the following month with news that he had been promoted to command of the regiment and colonel upon the regimental commander's death. The regiment was reinforced by an [independent company of a hundred South Carolinians](/wiki/British_Army_Independent_Companies_in_South_Carolina "British Army Independent Companies in South Carolina") led by Captain [James Mackay](/wiki/James_Mackay_(British_Army_officer) "James Mackay (British Army officer)"); his royal commission outranked Washington's and a conflict of command ensued. On July 3, a French force attacked with 900 men, and the [ensuing battle](/wiki/Battle_of_Fort_Necessity "Battle of Fort Necessity") ended in Washington's surrender.[\\[26\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling200923\'9625Ellis200415\'9617-29) He signed a surrender document in which he unwittingly took responsibility for "assassinating" Jumonville, later blaming the translator for not properly translating it.[\\[27\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling200924\'9625Chernow201042\'9645-30)\
\
In the aftermath, Colonel [James Innes](/wiki/James_Innes_(North_Carolina) "James Innes (North Carolina)") took command of intercolonial forces, the Virginia Regiment was divided, and Washington was offered a captaincy in one of the newly formed regiments. He refused, however, as it would have been a demotion and instead resigned his commission.[\\[28\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling200926-31) The "Jumonville affair" became the incident which ignited the [French and Indian War](/wiki/French_and_Indian_War "French and Indian War"), later to become part of the [Seven Years' War](/wiki/Seven_Years%27_War "Seven Years' War").[\\[29\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling200919\'9624Ellis200413Alden199613\'9615-32)[\\[30\\]](#cite_note-33)\
\
[![Washington on horseback in the middle of a battle scene with other soldiers](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Washington_the_soldier.jpg/260px-Washington_the_soldier.jpg)](/wiki/File:Washington_the_soldier.jpg)\
\
_Washington the Soldier_, an 1834 portrait of Washington on horseback during the [Battle of the Monongahela](/wiki/Battle_of_the_Monongahela "Battle of the Monongahela")\
\
In 1755, Washington served voluntarily as an aide to General [Edward Braddock](/wiki/Edward_Braddock "Edward Braddock"), who led a British [expedition](/wiki/Braddock_Expedition "Braddock Expedition") to expel the French from Fort Duquesne and the [Ohio Country](/wiki/Ohio_Country "Ohio Country").[\\[31\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow201053-34) On Washington's recommendation, Braddock split the army into one main column and a lightly equipped "flying column".[\\[32\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlden199637Ferling198835\'9636-35) Suffering from severe [dysentery](/wiki/Dysentery "Dysentery"), Washington was left behind, and when he rejoined Braddock at Monongahela the French and their Indian allies [ambushed](/wiki/Battle_of_the_Monongahela "Battle of the Monongahela") the divided army. Two-thirds of the British force became casualties, including the mortally wounded Braddock. Under the command of Lieutenant Colonel [Thomas Gage](/wiki/Thomas_Gage "Thomas Gage"), Washington, still very ill, rallied the survivors and formed a rear guard, allowing the remnants of the force to disengage and retreat.[\\[33\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlden199637\'9646Ferling198835\'9636Chernow201057\'9658-36)\
\
During the engagement, he had two horses shot from under him, and his hat and coat were bullet-pierced.[\\[34\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFitzpatrick1936511-37) His conduct under fire redeemed his reputation among critics of his command in the Battle of Fort Necessity,[\\[35\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling200928\'9630-38) but he was not included by the succeeding commander (Colonel Thomas Dunbar) in planning subsequent operations.[\\[36\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlden199637\'9646-39)\
\
The Virginia Regiment was reconstituted in August 1755, and Dinwiddie appointed Washington its commander, again with the rank of colonel. Washington clashed over seniority almost immediately, this time with [John Dagworthy](/wiki/John_Dagworthy "John Dagworthy"), another captain of superior royal rank, who commanded a detachment of Marylanders at the regiment's headquarters in [Fort Cumberland](/wiki/Fort_Cumberland_(Maryland) "Fort Cumberland (Maryland)").[\\[37\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllis200424Ferling200930\'9631-40) Washington, impatient for an offensive against Fort Duquesne, was convinced Braddock would have granted him a royal commission and pressed his case in February 1756 with Braddock's successor as [Commander-in-Chief](/wiki/Commander-in-Chief,_North_America "Commander-in-Chief, North America"), [William Shirley](/wiki/William_Shirley "William Shirley"), and again in January 1757 with Shirley's successor, [Lord Loudoun](/wiki/John_Campbell,_4th_Earl_of_Loudoun "John Campbell, 4th Earl of Loudoun"). Shirley ruled in Washington's favor only in the matter of Dagworthy; Loudoun humiliated Washington, refused him a royal commission and agreed only to relieve him of the responsibility of manning Fort Cumberland.[\\[38\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling200931\'9632,_38\'9639-41)\
\
In 1758, the Virginia Regiment was assigned to the British [Forbes Expedition](/wiki/Forbes_Expedition "Forbes Expedition") to capture Fort Duquesne.[\\[d\\]](#cite_note-43)[\\[40\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlexner1965194Fitzpatrick1936512-44) Washington disagreed with General [John Forbes'](/wiki/John_Forbes_(British_Army_officer) "John Forbes (British Army officer)") tactics and chosen route.[\\[41\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFitzpatrick1936512Chernow201089\'9690Flexner1965194,_206\'96207-45) Forbes nevertheless made Washington a [brevet](/wiki/Brevet_(military) "Brevet (military)") brigadier general and gave him command of one of the three brigades that would assault the fort. The French had abandoned the fort and the valley before the assault, however, and Washington only saw a friendly fire incident which left 14 dead and 26 injured. Frustrated, he resigned his commission soon afterwards and returned to Mount Vernon.[\\[42\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling200943Chernow201090\'9691Lengel200575\'9676,_81-46)\
\
Under Washington, the Virginia Regiment had defended 300 miles (480\'a0km) of frontier against twenty Indian attacks in ten months.[\\[43\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFitzpatrick1936511\'96512Flexner1965138Fischer200415\'9616Ellis200438-47) He increased the professionalism of the regiment as it grew from 300 to 1,000 men, and Virginia's frontier population suffered less than other colonies. Though he failed to realize a royal commission, he gained self-confidence, leadership skills, and knowledge of British military tactics. The destructive competition Washington witnessed among colonial politicians fostered his later support of a strong central government.[\\[44\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow201092\'9693Ferling200232\'9633-48)\
\
Marriage, civilian, and political life (1755\'961775)\
--------------------------------------------------\
\
Main article: [George Washington's political evolution](/wiki/George_Washington%27s_political_evolution "George Washington's political evolution")\
\
.mw-parser-output .tmulti .multiimageinner\{display:flex;flex-direction:column\}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow\{display:flex;flex-direction:row;clear:left;flex-wrap:wrap;width:100%;box-sizing:border-box\}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle\{margin:1px;float:left\}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .theader\{clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;align-self:center;background-color:transparent;width:100%\}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbcaption\{background-color:transparent\}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-left\{text-align:left\}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-right\{text-align:right\}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-center\{text-align:center\}@media all and (max-width:720px)\{.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbinner\{width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;max-width:none!important;align-items:center\}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow\{justify-content:center\}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle\{float:none!important;max-width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;text-align:center\}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle .thumbcaption\{text-align:left\}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow>.thumbcaption\{text-align:center\}\}\
\
[![Painting of Washington, by Charles Wilson Peale, standing in a formal pose, in a colonel's uniform, right hand inserted in shirt.](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Washington_1772.jpg/168px-Washington_1772.jpg)](/wiki/File:Washington_1772.jpg)\
\
_Colonel George Washington_, a 1772 portrait by [Charles Willson Peale](/wiki/Charles_Willson_Peale "Charles Willson Peale")\
\
[![A mezzotint of Martha Washington, standing, wearing a formal gown, based on a 1757 portrait by John Wollaston](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Martha_Dandridge_Custis_crop.jpg/146px-Martha_Dandridge_Custis_crop.jpg)](/wiki/File:Martha_Dandridge_Custis_crop.jpg)\
\
_Martha Dandridge Custis_, a 1757 portrait by [John Wollaston](/wiki/John_Wollaston_(painter) "John Wollaston (painter)")\
\
On January 6, 1759, Washington, at age 26, married [Martha Dandridge Custis](/wiki/Martha_Washington "Martha Washington"), the 27-year-old widow of wealthy plantation owner [Daniel Parke Custis](/wiki/Daniel_Parke_Custis "Daniel Parke Custis"). The marriage took place at Martha's estate; she was intelligent, gracious, and experienced in managing a planter's estate, and the couple had a happy marriage.[\\[45\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling200233\'9634Wiencek200369-49) They moved to Mount Vernon, near [Alexandria](/wiki/Alexandria,_Virginia "Alexandria, Virginia"), where he lived as a planter of tobacco and wheat and emerged as a political figure.[\\[46\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow201097\'9698Fischer200414-50)\
\
Washington's 1751 bout with smallpox is thought to have rendered him sterile, though it is equally likely that "Martha may have sustained injury during the birth of Patsy, her final child, making additional births impossible."[\\[47\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010103-51) The couple lamented not having any children together.[\\[e\\]](#cite_note-54)[\\[50\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010103Flexner197442\'9643-55) Despite this, the two raised Martha's two children [John Parke Custis](/wiki/John_Parke_Custis "John Parke Custis") (Jacky) and [Martha Parke Custis](/wiki/Martha_Parke_Custis "Martha Parke Custis") (Patsy), and later Jacky's two youngest children [Eleanor Parke Custis](/wiki/Eleanor_Parke_Custis_Lewis "Eleanor Parke Custis Lewis") (Nelly) and [George Washington Parke Custis](/wiki/George_Washington_Parke_Custis "George Washington Parke Custis") (Washy), along with numerous nieces and nephews.[\\[51\\]](#cite_note-56)\
\
The marriage gave Washington control over Martha's one-third [dower](/wiki/Dower "Dower") interest in the 18,000-acre (7,300\'a0ha) [Custis estate](/wiki/Daniel_Parke_Custis#Estate "Daniel Parke Custis"), and he managed the remaining two-thirds for Martha's children; the estate also included 84 slaves. As a result, he became one of the wealthiest men in Virginia, which increased his social standing.[\\[52\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWiencek20039\'9610,_67\'9669,_80\'9681-57)\
\
At Washington's urging, Governor [Lord Botetourt](/wiki/Norborne_Berkeley,_4th_Baron_Botetourt "Norborne Berkeley, 4th Baron Botetourt") fulfilled Dinwiddie's 1754 promise of land bounties to all-volunteer militia during the French and Indian War.[\\[53\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTERasmussenTilton1999100Chernow2010184-58) In late 1770, Washington inspected the lands in the [Ohio](/wiki/Ohio_River "Ohio River") and [Great Kanawha](/wiki/Kanawha_River "Kanawha River") regions, and he engaged surveyor [William Crawford](/wiki/William_Crawford_(soldier) "William Crawford (soldier)") to subdivide it. Crawford allotted 23,200 acres (9,400\'a0ha) to Washington; Washington told the veterans that their land was hilly and unsuitable for farming, and he agreed to purchase 20,147 acres (8,153\'a0ha), leaving some feeling they had been duped.[\\[54\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling200244\'9645Grizzard2002135\'96137-59) He also doubled the size of Mount Vernon to 6,500 acres (2,600\'a0ha) and, by 1775, had increased its slave population by more than a hundred.[\\[55\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllis200441\'9642,_48-60)\
\
As a respected military hero and large landowner, Washington held local offices and was elected to the Virginia provincial legislature, representing Frederick County in the House of Burgesses for seven years beginning in 1758.[\\[55\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllis200441\'9642,_48-60) He first ran for the seat in 1755 but was soundly beaten by Hugh West.[\\[56\\]](#cite_note-61)[\\[57\\]](#cite_note-62) When he ran in 1758, Washington plied voters with beer, brandy, and other beverages. Despite being away serving on the Forbes Expedition, he won the election with roughly 40 percent of the vote, defeating three opponents with the help of local supporters.[\\[58\\]](#cite_note-63)[\\[59\\]](#cite_note-64)\
\
Early in his legislative career, Washington rarely spoke or even attended legislative sessions. He would later become a prominent critic of Britain's taxation policy and mercantilist policies towards the American colonies and became more politically active starting in the 1760s.[\\[60\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling200949\'9654,_68-65)\
\
Washington imported luxuries and other goods from England, paying for them by exporting tobacco. His profligate spending combined with low tobacco prices left him \'a31,800 in debt by 1764, prompting him to diversify his holdings.[\\[61\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllis200449\'9650-66) In 1765, because of erosion and other soil problems, he changed Mount Vernon's primary cash crop from tobacco to wheat and expanded operations to include corn [flour milling](/wiki/George_Washington%27s_Gristmill "George Washington's Gristmill") and fishing.[\\[62\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010141-67)[\\[63\\]](#cite_note-68)\
\
Washington soon was counted among the political and social elite in Virginia. From 1768 to 1775, he invited some 2,000 guests to [Mount Vernon](/wiki/Mount_Vernon "Mount Vernon"), mostly those whom he considered people of rank, and was known to be exceptionally cordial toward guests.[\\[64\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010122-69) Washington also took time for leisure with fox hunting, fishing, dances, theater, cards, backgammon, and billiards.[\\[65\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling200243\'9644Ellis200444-70)\
\
Washington's stepdaughter Patsy suffered from epileptic attacks from age 12, and she died at [Mount Vernon](/wiki/Mount_Vernon "Mount Vernon") in 1773. The following day, he wrote to [Burwell Bassett](/wiki/Burwell_Bassett_Sr. "Burwell Bassett Sr."): "It is easier to conceive, than to describe, the distress of this Family".[\\[66\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010161-71) He canceled all business activity and remained with Martha every night for three months.[\\[67\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHigginbotham2001154-72)\
\
### Opposition to the British Parliament and Crown\
\
Further information: [American Revolution](/wiki/American_Revolution "American Revolution") and [George Washington in the American Revolution](/wiki/George_Washington_in_the_American_Revolution "George Washington in the American Revolution")\
\
Washington played a central role before and during the American Revolution. His distrust of the British military had begun when he was passed over for promotion into the Regular Army. Opposed to taxes imposed by the British Parliament on the Colonies without [proper representation](/wiki/Virtual_representation "Virtual representation"),[\\[68\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010136-73) he and other colonists were also angered by the [Royal Proclamation of 1763](/wiki/Royal_Proclamation_of_1763 "Royal Proclamation of 1763") which banned American settlement west of the [Allegheny Mountains](/wiki/Allegheny_Mountains "Allegheny Mountains") and protected the [British fur trade](/wiki/North_American_fur_trade "North American fur trade").[\\[69\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010137,_148Taylor201661,_75-74)\
\
Washington believed the [Stamp Act 1765](/wiki/Stamp_Act_1765 "Stamp Act 1765") was an "Act of Oppression" and celebrated its repeal the following year.[\\[f\\]](#cite_note-76) In March 1766, Parliament passed the [Declaratory Act](/wiki/Declaratory_Act "Declaratory Act") asserting that Parliamentary law superseded colonial law.[\\[71\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor2016103-77) In the late 1760s, the interference of the British Crown in American lucrative western land speculation spurred the American Revolution.[\\[72\\]](#cite_note-Lehrman_Institute_(June_22,_2021)-78) Washington was a prosperous land speculator, and in 1767, he encouraged "adventures" to acquire backcountry western lands.[\\[72\\]](#cite_note-Lehrman_Institute_(June_22,_2021)-78) Washington helped lead widespread protests against the [Townshend Acts](/wiki/Townshend_Acts "Townshend Acts") passed by Parliament in 1767, and he introduced a proposal in May 1769 which urged Virginians to boycott British goods; the Acts were mostly repealed in 1770.[\\[73\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFreeman1968174\'96176Taylor201675-79)\
\
Parliament sought to punish Massachusetts colonists for their role in the [Boston Tea Party](/wiki/Boston_Tea_Party "Boston Tea Party") in 1774 by passing the [Coercive Acts](/wiki/Intolerable_Acts "Intolerable Acts"), which Washington saw as "an invasion of our rights and privileges".[\\[74\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTERandall1997262Chernow2010166Taylor2016119-80) He said Americans must not submit to acts of tyranny since "custom and use shall make us as tame and abject slaves, as the blacks we rule over with such arbitrary sway".[\\[75\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlden1996101-81) That July, he and [George Mason](/wiki/George_Mason "George Mason") drafted a [list of resolutions](/wiki/Fairfax_Resolves "Fairfax Resolves") for the Fairfax County committee, including a call to end the [Atlantic slave trade](/wiki/Atlantic_slave_trade "Atlantic slave trade"), which were adopted.[\\[76\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010167-82)\
\
On August 1, Washington attended the [First Virginia Convention](/wiki/Virginia_Conventions "Virginia Conventions"). There, he was selected as a delegate to the [First Continental Congress](/wiki/First_Continental_Congress "First Continental Congress").[\\[77\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling1988100FordHuntFitzpatrick1904v._19,_p._11-83) As tensions rose in 1774, he helped train militias in Virginia and organized enforcement of the [Continental Association](/wiki/Continental_Association "Continental Association") boycott of British goods instituted by the Congress.[\\[78\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling1988108Taylor2016126\'96127-84)\
\
The [American Revolutionary War](/wiki/American_Revolutionary_War "American Revolutionary War") broke out on April 19, 1775, with the [Battles of Lexington and Concord](/wiki/Battles_of_Lexington_and_Concord "Battles of Lexington and Concord") and the [Siege of Boston](/wiki/Siege_of_Boston "Siege of Boston").[\\[79\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor2016132-85) Upon hearing the news, Washington was "sobered and dismayed",[\\[80\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010181-86) and he hastily departed Mount Vernon on May 4, 1775, to join the [Second Continental Congress](/wiki/Second_Continental_Congress "Second Continental Congress") in [Philadelphia](/wiki/Philadelphia "Philadelphia").[\\[81\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010182-87)\
\
Commander in chief (1775\'961783)\
------------------------------\
\
Further information: [Military career of George Washington](/wiki/Military_career_of_George_Washington "Military career of George Washington") and [Washington's aides-de-camp](/wiki/Washington%27s_aides-de-camp "Washington's aides-de-camp")\
\
[![Formal painting of General George Washington, standing in uniform, as commander of the Continental Army](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/George_Washington%2C_1776.jpg/220px-George_Washington%2C_1776.jpg)](/wiki/File:George_Washington,_1776.jpg)\
\
_General Washington, Commander of the Continental Army_, a 1776 portrait by [Charles Willson Peale](/wiki/Charles_Willson_Peale "Charles Willson Peale")\
\
On June 14, 1775, [Congress](/wiki/Second_Continental_Congress "Second Continental Congress") created the [Continental Army](/wiki/Continental_Army "Continental Army") and [John Adams](/wiki/John_Adams "John Adams") nominated Washington as its [commander-in-chief](/wiki/Commander-in-chief "Commander-in-chief"), mainly because of his military experience and the belief that a Virginian would better unite the colonies. He was unanimously elected by Congress the next day.[\\[82\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor2016132\'96133Ellis200467\'9668Chernow2010185\'96186Fitzpatrick1936514-88) Washington appeared before Congress in uniform and gave an acceptance speech on June 16, declining a salary, though he was later reimbursed expenses.[\\[83\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTERasmussenTilton1999294Fitzpatrick1936514Taylor2016141\'96142Ferling200986\'9687-89)\
\
Washington was commissioned on June 19 and officially appointed by Congress as "General & Commander in chief of the army of the [United Colonies](/wiki/United_Colonies "United Colonies") and of all the forces raised or to be raised by them".[\\[84\\]](#cite_note-90) He was instructed to take charge of the [Siege of Boston](/wiki/Siege_of_Boston "Siege of Boston") on June 22, 1775.[\\[85\\]](#cite_note-91)\
\
Congress chose his primary staff officers, including Major General [Artemas Ward](/wiki/Artemas_Ward "Artemas Ward"), Adjutant General [Horatio Gates](/wiki/Horatio_Gates "Horatio Gates"), Major General [Charles Lee](/wiki/Charles_Lee_(general) "Charles Lee (general)"), Major General [Philip Schuyler](/wiki/Philip_Schuyler "Philip Schuyler"), and Major General [Nathanael Greene](/wiki/Nathanael_Greene "Nathanael Greene"). [\\[86\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010190\'96191Ferling2002108-92) [Henry Knox](/wiki/Henry_Knox "Henry Knox"), a young bookkeeper, impressed Adams and Washington with ordnance knowledge and was subsequently promoted to colonel and chief of artillery. Similarly, Washington was impressed by [Alexander Hamilton](/wiki/Alexander_Hamilton "Alexander Hamilton")'s intelligence and bravery. He would later promote him to colonel and appoint him his [aide-de-camp](/wiki/Aide-de-camp "Aide-de-camp").[\\[87\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling2002109\'96110Puls200831-93)\
\
Washington initially banned the enlistment of blacks, both free and enslaved, into the Continental Army. The British saw an opportunity to divide the colonies, and the colonial governor of Virginia issued [a proclamation](/wiki/Dunmore%27s_Proclamation "Dunmore's Proclamation"), which promised freedom to slaves if they joined the British.[\\[88\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan2000290\'96291-94) Desperate for manpower by late 1777, Washington relented and overturned his ban.[\\[89\\]](#cite_note-95) By the end of the war, around one-tenth of Washington's army were blacks.[\\[90\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor2016231-96) Following the British surrender, Washington sought to enforce terms of the preliminary [Treaty of Paris (1783)](/wiki/Treaty_of_Paris_(1783) "Treaty of Paris (1783)") by reclaiming slaves freed by the British and returning them to servitude. He arranged to make this request to [Sir Guy Carleton](/wiki/Guy_Carleton,_1st_Baron_Dorchester "Guy Carleton, 1st Baron Dorchester") on May 6, 1783. Instead, Carleton issued 3,000 freedom certificates and all former slaves in [New York City](/wiki/New_York_City "New York City") were able to leave before the city was evacuated by the British in late November 1783.[\\[91\\]](#cite_note-97)\
\
### Siege of Boston\
\
Main article: [Siege of Boston](/wiki/Siege_of_Boston "Siege of Boston")\
\
Early in 1775, in response to the growing rebellious movement, London sent British troops to occupy [Boston](/wiki/Boston "Boston"), led by General [Thomas Gage](/wiki/Thomas_Gage "Thomas Gage"), commander of British forces in America.[\\[92\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor2016121\'96123-98) They set up fortifications, making the city impervious to attack. Local militias surrounded the city and effectively trapped the British troops, resulting in a standoff.[\\[93\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor2016121\'96122,_143-99)\
\
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Washington_taking_command_of_the_American_Army_at_Cambridge%2C_1775_-_NARA_-_532874.jpg/240px-Washington_taking_command_of_the_American_Army_at_Cambridge%2C_1775_-_NARA_-_532874.jpg)](/wiki/File:Washington_taking_command_of_the_American_Army_at_Cambridge,_1775_-_NARA_-_532874.jpg)\
\
Washington arriving in [Boston](/wiki/Boston "Boston") on July 2, 1775, to take command of the [Continental Army](/wiki/Continental_Army "Continental Army")\
\
As Washington headed for Boston, word of his march preceded him, and he was greeted everywhere; gradually, he became a symbol of the Patriot cause.[\\[94\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010193-100) Upon arrival on July 2, 1775, two weeks after the [Battle of Bunker Hill](/wiki/Battle_of_Bunker_Hill "Battle of Bunker Hill"), he set up headquarters in [Cambridge](/wiki/Cambridge,_Massachusetts "Cambridge, Massachusetts"). When he went to inspect the army, he found undisciplined militia.[\\[95\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor2016143-101) After consultation, he initiated [Benjamin Franklin](/wiki/Benjamin_Franklin "Benjamin Franklin")'s suggested reforms: drilling the soldiers and imposing strict discipline.[\\[96\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIsaacson2003303Ferling2002112Taylor2016143Fitzpatrick1936514-102) Washington ordered his officers to identify the skills of recruits to ensure military effectiveness, while removing incompetent officers.[\\[97\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling2002112\'96113,_116-103) He petitioned Gage, his former superior, to release captured Patriot officers from prison and treat them humanely.[\\[98\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow201057,_160,_166,_201-104) In October 1775, King [George III](/wiki/George_III "George III") declared that the colonies were in open rebellion and relieved Gage of command for incompetence, replacing him with General [William Howe](/wiki/William_Howe,_5th_Viscount_Howe "William Howe, 5th Viscount Howe").[\\[99\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010208Taylor2016133\'96135-105)\
\
The Continental Army, reduced to only 9,600 men by January 1776 due to expiring short-term enlistments, had to be supplemented with militia. Soon, they were joined by [Knox with heavy artillery](/wiki/Noble_train_of_artillery "Noble train of artillery") captured from [Fort Ticonderoga](/wiki/Capture_of_Fort_Ticonderoga "Capture of Fort Ticonderoga").[\\[100\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTELengel2005124\'96126Ferling2002116\'96119-106) When the [Charles River](/wiki/Charles_River "Charles River") froze over, Washington was eager to cross and storm Boston, but General Gates and others were opposed to untrained militia striking well-garrisoned fortifications. Instead, he agreed to [secure the Dorchester Heights](/wiki/Fortification_of_Dorchester_Heights "Fortification of Dorchester Heights"), 100 feet above Boston, with Knox's artillery to try to force the British out.[\\[101\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling2009100-107)\
\
On March 9, under cover of darkness, Washington's troops bombarded British ships in Boston harbor. On March 17, 9,000 British troops and [Loyalists](/wiki/Loyalist_(American_Revolution) "Loyalist (American Revolution)") began a chaotic ten-day evacuation aboard 120 ships. Soon after, Washington entered the city with 500 men, with explicit orders not to plunder the city.[\\[102\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHenderson200947-108) He refrained from exerting military authority in Boston, leaving civilian matters in the hands of local authorities.[\\[g\\]](#cite_note-110)[\\[104\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010227\'96228Lengel2005124\'96126Ferling2002116\'96119Taylor2016144,_153\'96154-111)\
\
### New York and New Jersey\
\
Main article: [New York and New Jersey Campaign](/wiki/New_York_and_New_Jersey_Campaign "New York and New Jersey Campaign")\
\
#### Battle of Long Island\
\
Main article: [Battle of Long Island](/wiki/Battle_of_Long_Island "Battle of Long Island")\
\
[![Painting by Alonzo Chappel, 1858, showing the frantic battle scene of the Battle of Long Island, with smoke in the background](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Battleoflongisland.jpg/220px-Battleoflongisland.jpg)](/wiki/File:Battleoflongisland.jpg)\
\
_Battle of Long Island_, an 1858 painting by [Alonzo Chappel](/wiki/Alonzo_Chappel "Alonzo Chappel")\
\
After the victory at Boston, Washington correctly guessed that the British would return to [New York City](/wiki/New_York_City "New York City"), a Loyalist stronghold, and retaliate. He arrived there on April 13, 1776, and ordered the construction of fortifications to thwart the expected British attack. He also ordered his occupying forces to treat civilians and their property with respect, to avoid the abuses Bostonians suffered at the hands of British troops.[\\[105\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010229\'96230-112)\
\
Howe transported his resupplied army, with the British fleet, from [Halifax](/wiki/Halifax,_Nova_Scotia "Halifax, Nova Scotia") to [New York City](/wiki/New_York_City "New York City"). [George Germain](/wiki/George_Germain,_1st_Viscount_Sackville "George Germain, 1st Viscount Sackville"), who ran the British war effort in England, believed it could be won with one "decisive blow".[\\[106\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010235-113) The British forces, including more than a hundred ships and thousands of troops, began arriving on [Staten Island](/wiki/Staten_Island "Staten Island") on July\'a02 to lay siege to the city.[\\[107\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFitzpatrick1936514\'96515Taylor2016162\'96163-114) After the [Declaration of Independence](/wiki/United_States_Declaration_of_Independence "United States Declaration of Independence") was unanimously adopted on July 4, Washington informed his troops on July\'a09 that Congress had declared the united colonies to be "free and independent states".[\\[108\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010237-115)\
\
Howe's troop strength totaled 32,000 regulars and [Hessian auxiliaries](/wiki/Hessian_(soldier) "Hessian (soldier)"), and Washington's consisted of 23,000, mostly raw recruits and militia.[\\[109\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010244\'96245Taylor2016162\'96163-116) In August, Howe landed 20,000 troops at [Gravesend, Brooklyn](/wiki/Gravesend,_Brooklyn "Gravesend, Brooklyn"), and approached Washington's fortifications Opposing his generals, Washington chose to fight, based on inaccurate information that Howe's army had only 8,000-plus troops.[\\[110\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllis200495\'9696Chernow2010244-117) In the [Battle of Long Island](/wiki/Battle_of_Long_Island "Battle of Long Island"), Howe assaulted Washington's flank and inflicted 1,500 Patriot casualties, the British suffering 400.[\\[111\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor2016164-118) Washington retreated, instructing General [William Heath](/wiki/William_Heath "William Heath") to acquire river craft. On August 30, General [William Stirling](/wiki/William_Alexander,_Lord_Stirling "William Alexander, Lord Stirling") held off the British and gave cover while the army crossed the [East River](/wiki/East_River "East River") under darkness to [Manhattan](/wiki/Manhattan "Manhattan") without loss of life or materiel, although Alexander was captured.[\\[112\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCullough2005186\'96195-119) Howe was emboldened by his Long Island victory and dispatched Washington as "George Washington, Esq." in futility to negotiate peace. Washington declined, demanding to be addressed with diplomatic protocol, as general and fellow belligerent, not as a "rebel", lest his men be hanged as such if captured.[\\[113\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010240Davis197593\'9694Taylor2016164-120) The [Royal Navy](/wiki/Royal_Navy "Royal Navy") bombarded the unstable earthworks on lower Manhattan Island.[\\[114\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor2016165-121) Despite misgivings, Washington heeded the advice of Generals Greene and [Putnam](/wiki/Israel_Putnam "Israel Putnam") to defend [Fort Washington](/wiki/Fort_Washington_(Manhattan) "Fort Washington (Manhattan)"). They were unable to hold it; Washington abandoned the fort and ordered his army north to the [White Plains](/wiki/Battle_of_White_Plains "Battle of White Plains").[\\[115\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavis1975136Chernow2010257-122)\
\
Howe's pursuit forced Washington to retreat across the [Hudson River](/wiki/Hudson_River "Hudson River") to [Fort Lee](/wiki/Fort_Lee_Historic_Park "Fort Lee Historic Park") to avoid encirclement. Howe landed his troops on Manhattan in November and [captured Fort Washington](/wiki/Battle_of_Fort_Washington "Battle of Fort Washington"), inflicting high casualties on the Americans. Washington was responsible for delaying the retreat, though he blamed Congress and General Greene. Loyalists in New York City considered Howe a liberator and spread a rumor that Washington had set fire to the city.[\\[116\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlden1996137Taylor2016165-123) Patriot morale reached its lowest when Lee was captured.[\\[117\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor2016166\'96167Farner199624-124) Now reduced to 5,400 troops, Washington's army retreated through [New Jersey](/wiki/Province_of_New_Jersey "Province of New Jersey"), and Howe broke off pursuit to set up winter quarters in New York.[\\[118\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFischer2004224\'96226Taylor2016166\'96169-125)\
\
#### Crossing the Delaware, Trenton, and Princeton\
\
Main articles: [George Washington's crossing of the Delaware River](/wiki/George_Washington%27s_crossing_of_the_Delaware_River "George Washington's crossing of the Delaware River"), [Battle of Trenton](/wiki/Battle_of_Trenton "Battle of Trenton"), [Battle of the Assunpink Creek](/wiki/Battle_of_the_Assunpink_Creek "Battle of the Assunpink Creek"), and [Battle of Princeton](/wiki/Battle_of_Princeton "Battle of Princeton")\
\
[![Famous 1851 painting by Emanuel Leutze, depicting Washington, standing in a boat with his troops, crossing the icy Delaware River, with soldiers pushing away chunks of ice](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Washington_Crossing_the_Delaware_by_Emanuel_Leutze%2C_MMA-NYC%2C_1851.jpg/220px-Washington_Crossing_the_Delaware_by_Emanuel_Leutze%2C_MMA-NYC%2C_1851.jpg)](/wiki/File:Washington_Crossing_the_Delaware_by_Emanuel_Leutze,_MMA-NYC,_1851.jpg)\
\
[_Washington Crossing the Delaware_](/wiki/Washington_Crossing_the_Delaware_(1851_paintings) "Washington Crossing the Delaware (1851 paintings)"), an 1851 portrait by [Emanuel Leutze](/wiki/Emanuel_Leutze "Emanuel Leutze")\
\
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/1819_Passage_OfThe_Delaware_byThomasSully_MFABoston.jpeg/220px-1819_Passage_OfThe_Delaware_byThomasSully_MFABoston.jpeg)](/wiki/File:1819_Passage_OfThe_Delaware_byThomasSully_MFABoston.jpeg)\
\
_[The Passage of the Delaware](/wiki/The_Passage_of_the_Delaware "The Passage of the Delaware")_, an 1819 portrait by [Thomas Sully](/wiki/Thomas_Sully "Thomas Sully")\
\
[![Painting showing Washington on horseback, accepting the surrender of Hessian troops after the Battle at Trenton](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/The_Capture_of_the_Hessians_at_Trenton_December_26_1776.jpeg/220px-The_Capture_of_the_Hessians_at_Trenton_December_26_1776.jpeg)](/wiki/File:The_Capture_of_the_Hessians_at_Trenton_December_26_1776.jpeg)\
\
_[The Capture of the Hessians at Trenton, December 26, 1776](/wiki/The_Capture_of_the_Hessians_at_Trenton,_December_26,_1776 "The Capture of the Hessians at Trenton, December 26, 1776")_, a [John Trumbull](/wiki/John_Trumbull "John Trumbull") portrait depicting the [Battle of Trenton](/wiki/Battle_of_Trenton "Battle of Trenton")\
\
Washington crossed the [Delaware River](/wiki/Delaware_River "Delaware River") into [Pennsylvania](/wiki/Pennsylvania "Pennsylvania"), where Lee's replacement General [John Sullivan](/wiki/John_Sullivan_(general) "John Sullivan (general)") joined him with 2,000 more troops.[\\[119\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor2016166\'96167,_169-126) The future of the Continental Army was in doubt due to lack of supplies, a harsh winter, expiring enlistments, and desertions. Washington was disappointed that many New Jersey residents were Loyalists or skeptical about independence.[\\[120\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKetchum1999235Chernow2010264-127)\
\
Howe split up his army and posted a [Hessian](/wiki/Hessian_(soldier) "Hessian (soldier)") garrison at [Trenton](/wiki/Trenton,_New_Jersey "Trenton, New Jersey") to hold western New Jersey and the east shore of the Delaware.[\\[121\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor2016169-128) Desperate for a victory, Washington and his generals devised a surprise attack on Trenton. The army was to cross the Delaware in three divisions: one led by Washington (2,400 troops), another by General [James Ewing](/wiki/James_Ewing_(Pennsylvania_politician) "James Ewing (Pennsylvania politician)") (700), and the third by Colonel [John Cadwalader](/wiki/John_Cadwalader_(general) "John Cadwalader (general)") (1,500). The force was to then split, with Washington taking the [Pennington](/wiki/Pennington,_New_Jersey "Pennington, New Jersey") Road and General Sullivan traveling south on the river's edge.[\\[122\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010270\'96273-129)\
\
Washington ordered a 60-mile search for [Durham boats](/wiki/Durham_boat "Durham boat") to transport his army, and the destruction of vessels that could be used by the British.[\\[123\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010270\'96272Randall1997319-130) He personally risked capture while staking out the Jersey shoreline alone leading up to the crossing. Washington [crossed the Delaware](/wiki/George_Washington%27s_crossing_of_the_Delaware_River "George Washington's crossing of the Delaware River") on [Christmas](/wiki/Christmas "Christmas") night, 1776.[\\[124\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWillcoxArnstein1988164-131) His men followed across the ice-obstructed river from [McConkey's Ferry](/wiki/Washington_Crossing_Historic_Park#McConkey's_Ferry "Washington Crossing Historic Park"), with 40 men per vessel. The wind churned up the waters, and they were pelted with hail, but by 3:00\'a0a.m. on December 26, they made it across with no losses.[\\[125\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010273-132) Knox was delayed, managing frightened horses and about 18 field guns on flat-bottomed ferries. Cadwalader and Ewing failed to cross due to the ice and heavy currents. Once Knox arrived, Washington proceeded to Trenton, rather than risk being spotted returning his army to Pennsylvania.[\\[126\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010273\'96274Fischer2004215\'96219Taylor2016171-133)\
\
The troops spotted Hessian positions a mile from Trenton, so Washington split his force into two columns, rallying his men: "Soldiers keep by your officers. For God's sake, keep by your officers." The two columns were separated at the Birmingham crossroads. General Greene's column took the upper Ferry Road, led by Washington, and General Sullivan's column advanced on River Road.[\\[127\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFischer2004228\'96230-134) The Americans marched in sleet and snowfall. Many were shoeless with bloodied feet, and two died of exposure. At sunrise, Washington, aided by Colonel Knox and artillery, led his men in a surprise attack on the unsuspecting Hessians and their commander, Colonel [Johann Rall](/wiki/Johann_Rall "Johann Rall"). The Hessians had 22 killed, including Colonel Rall, 83 wounded, and 850 captured with supplies.[\\[128\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010270,_275\'96276Ferling2002146\'96147Fischer2004170,_232\'96234,_254,_405-135)\
\
Washington retreated across the Delaware to Pennsylvania and returned to New Jersey on January 3, 1777, launching [an attack](/wiki/Battle_of_Princeton "Battle of Princeton") on British regulars at [Princeton](/wiki/Princeton,_New_Jersey "Princeton, New Jersey"), with 40 Americans killed or wounded and 273 British killed or captured.[\\[129\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFischer2004254Ketchum1999306\'96307Alden1996146-136) American Generals [Hugh Mercer](/wiki/Hugh_Mercer "Hugh Mercer") and John Cadwalader were being driven back by the British when Mercer was mortally wounded. Washington arrived and led the men in a counterattack which advanced to within 30 yards (27\'a0m) of the British line.[\\[130\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlden1996145-137)\
\
Some British troops retreated after a brief stand, while others took refuge in [Nassau Hall](/wiki/Nassau_Hall "Nassau Hall"), which became the target of Colonel [Alexander Hamilton](/wiki/Alexander_Hamilton "Alexander Hamilton")'s cannons. Washington's troops charged, the British surrendered in less than an hour, and 194 soldiers laid down their arms.[\\[131\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKetchum1999361\'96364Fischer2004339Chernow2010276\'96278-138) Howe retreated to New York City where his army remained inactive until early the next year.[\\[132\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor2016172-139) Washington took up winter headquarters in [Jacob Arnold's Tavern](/wiki/Arnold%27s_Tavern "Arnold's Tavern") in [Morristown, New Jersey](/wiki/Morristown,_New_Jersey "Morristown, New Jersey"),[\\[133\\]](#cite_note-140) while he received munition from the [Hibernia mines](/wiki/Hibernia_mines "Hibernia mines").[\\[134\\]](#cite_note-141) While in Morristown, Washington's troops disrupted British supply lines and expelled them from parts of New Jersey.[\\[135\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010285\'96286-142)\
\
During his stay in Morristown, Washington ordered the [inoculation](/wiki/Inoculation "Inoculation") of Continental troops against smallpox. This went against the wishes of the [Continental Congress](/wiki/Continental_Congress "Continental Congress") who had issued a proclamation prohibiting it, but Washington feared the spread of smallpox in the army. The mass inoculation proved successful, with only isolated infections occurring and no regiments incapacitated by the disease.[\\[136\\]](#cite_note-143)\
\
The British still controlled New York, and many Patriot soldiers did not re-enlist or deserted after the harsh winter campaign. Congress instituted greater rewards for re-enlisting and punishments for desertion to effect greater troop numbers.[\\[137\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFischer2004151-144) Strategically, Washington's victories at Trenton and Princeton were pivotal; they revived Patriot morale and quashed the British strategy of showing overwhelming force followed by offering generous terms, changing the course of the war.[\\[138\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor2016172Fischer2004367-145)[\\[124\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWillcoxArnstein1988164-131) In February 1777, word of the American victories reached London, and the British realized the Patriots were in a position to demand unconditional independence.[\\[139\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling2007188-146)\
\
### Philadelphia\
\
Main article: [Philadelphia campaign](/wiki/Philadelphia_campaign "Philadelphia campaign")\
\
#### Brandywine, Germantown, and Saratoga\
\
Main articles: [Battle of Brandywine](/wiki/Battle_of_Brandywine "Battle of Brandywine"), [Battle of Germantown](/wiki/Battle_of_Germantown "Battle of Germantown"), and [Battles of Saratoga](/wiki/Battles_of_Saratoga "Battles of Saratoga")\
\
In July 1777, British General [John Burgoyne](/wiki/John_Burgoyne "John Burgoyne") led the [Saratoga campaign](/wiki/Saratoga_campaign "Saratoga campaign") south from Quebec through [Lake Champlain](/wiki/Lake_Champlain "Lake Champlain") and [recaptured Fort Ticonderoga](/wiki/Siege_of_Fort_Ticonderoga_(1777) "Siege of Fort Ticonderoga (1777)") intending to divide [New England](/wiki/New_England "New England"), including control of the [Hudson River](/wiki/Hudson_River "Hudson River"). However, General Howe in British-occupied New York City blundered, taking his army south to [Philadelphia](/wiki/Philadelphia "Philadelphia") rather than up the [Hudson River](/wiki/Hudson_River "Hudson River") to join Burgoyne near [Albany](/wiki/Albany,_New_York "Albany, New York").[\\[140\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010300\'96301-147)\
\
Washington and [Gilbert du Motier, Marquis de Lafayette](/wiki/Gilbert_du_Motier,_Marquis_de_Lafayette "Gilbert du Motier, Marquis de Lafayette") rushed to Philadelphia to engage Howe. In the [Battle of Brandywine](/wiki/Battle_of_Brandywine "Battle of Brandywine"), on September 11, 1777, Howe outmaneuvered Washington and marched unopposed into the nation's capital at Philadelphia. A Patriot [attack](/wiki/Battle_of_Germantown "Battle of Germantown") failed against the British at [Germantown](/wiki/Germantown,_Philadelphia "Germantown, Philadelphia") in October.[\\[141\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTERandall1997340\'96341Chernow2010301\'96304-148)\
\
In [Upstate New York](/wiki/Upstate_New_York "Upstate New York"), the Patriots were led by General [Horatio Gates](/wiki/Horatio_Gates "Horatio Gates"). Concerned about Burgoyne's movements southward, Washington sent reinforcements north with Generals [Benedict Arnold](/wiki/Benedict_Arnold "Benedict Arnold"), his most aggressive field commander, and [Benjamin Lincoln](/wiki/Benjamin_Lincoln "Benjamin Lincoln"). On October 7, 1777, Burgoyne tried to take [Bemis Heights](/wiki/Battles_of_Saratoga "Battles of Saratoga") but was isolated from support by Howe. He was forced to retreat to Saratoga and ultimately surrendered after the [Battles of Saratoga](/wiki/Battles_of_Saratoga "Battles of Saratoga"). As Washington suspected, Gates' victory emboldened his critics.[\\[142\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010312\'96313-149)\
\
Biographer John Alden maintains, "It was inevitable that the defeats of Washington's forces and the concurrent victory of the forces in upper New York should be compared." Admiration for Washington was waning, including little credit from John Adams.[\\[143\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlden1996163-150)[\\[144\\]](#cite_note-151)\
\
#### Valley Forge and Monmouth\
\
Main articles: [Valley Forge](/wiki/Valley_Forge "Valley Forge") and [Battle of Monmouth](/wiki/Battle_of_Monmouth "Battle of Monmouth")\
\
[![Painting showing Washington and the Marquis de Lafayette on horseback in a winter setting, at Valley Forge](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Washington_and_Lafayette_at_Valley_Forge.jpg/220px-Washington_and_Lafayette_at_Valley_Forge.jpg)](/wiki/File:Washington_and_Lafayette_at_Valley_Forge.jpg)\
\
_Washington and Lafayette at Valley Forge_, a 1907 portrait by [John Ward Dunsmore](/wiki/John_Ward_Dunsmore "John Ward Dunsmore")\
\
[![Painting showing Washington on horseback, sword raised, in the midst of battle.](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/BattleofMonmouth.jpg/220px-BattleofMonmouth.jpg)](/wiki/File:BattleofMonmouth.jpg)\
\
_Washington Rallying the Troops at Monmouth_, an 1854 portrait by [Emanuel Leutze](/wiki/Emanuel_Leutze "Emanuel Leutze") depicting Washington at the [Battle of Monmouth](/wiki/Battle_of_Monmouth "Battle of Monmouth")\
\
Washington and his [Continental Army](/wiki/Continental_Army "Continental Army") of 11,000 men went into winter quarters at [Valley Forge](/wiki/Valley_Forge "Valley Forge") north of [Philadelphia](/wiki/Philadelphia "Philadelphia") in December 1777. There they lost between 2,000 and 3,000 men as a result of disease and lack of food, clothing, and shelter.[\\[145\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling2002186Alden1996165,_167Freedman200830-152) The British were comfortably quartered in Philadelphia, paying for supplies in [pounds sterling](/wiki/Pound_sterling "Pound sterling"), while Washington struggled with a devalued [American paper currency](/wiki/Early_American_currency "Early American currency"). The woodlands were soon exhausted of game. By February, Washington was facing lowered morale and increased desertions among his troops.[\\[146\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlden1996165-153)\
\
An [internal revolt](/wiki/Conway_Cabal "Conway Cabal") by his officers, led by Major General [Thomas Conway](/wiki/Thomas_Conway "Thomas Conway"), prompted some members of Congress to consider removing Washington from command. Washington's supporters resisted, and the matter was dropped after much deliberation.[\\[147\\]](#cite_note-154) Once the plot was exposed, Conway wrote an apology to Washington, resigned, and returned to France.[\\[148\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlexner1965138Randall1997354\'96355-155)\
\
Washington made repeated petitions to Congress for provisions. He received a congressional delegation to check the Army's conditions and expressed the urgency of the situation, proclaiming: "Something must be done. Important alterations must be made." He recommended that Congress expedite supplies, and Congress agreed to strengthen and fund the army's supply lines by reorganizing the commissary department. By late February, supplies began arriving.[\\[103\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFreedman200842-109) Meanwhile, Baron [Friedrich Wilhelm von Steuben](/wiki/Friedrich_Wilhelm_von_Steuben "Friedrich Wilhelm von Steuben")'s incessant drilling transformed Washington's recruits into a disciplined fighting force by the end of winter camp.[\\[149\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTERandall1997342,_356,_359Ferling2009172Alden1996168-156) For his services, Washington promoted Von Steuben to Major General and made him chief of staff.[\\[150\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010336-157)[\\[151\\]](#cite_note-158)\
\
In early 1778, the French responded to Burgoyne's defeat and entered into a [Treaty of Alliance](/wiki/Treaty_of_Alliance_(1778) "Treaty of Alliance (1778)") with the Americans. Congress ratified the treaty in May, which amounted to a French declaration of war against Britain.[\\[152\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor2016188-159) In May 1778, Howe resigned and was replaced by [Sir Henry Clinton](/wiki/Henry_Clinton_(British_Army_officer,_born_1730) "Henry Clinton (British Army officer, born 1730)").[\\[153\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling2007296-160)\
\
The British evacuated Philadelphia for New York that June and Washington summoned a war council of American and French generals. He chose a partial attack on the retreating British at the [Battle of Monmouth](/wiki/Battle_of_Monmouth "Battle of Monmouth"). Generals [Charles Lee](/wiki/Charles_Lee_(general) "Charles Lee (general)") and Lafayette moved with 4,000 men, without Washington's knowledge, and bungled their first attack on June 28. Washington relieved Lee and achieved a draw after an expansive battle. At nightfall, the British continued their retreat to New York, and Washington moved his army outside the city.[\\[154\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlden1996176\'96177Ferling2002195\'96198-161) Monmouth was Washington's last battle in the North.[\\[155\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010344-162)\
\
### West Point espionage\
\
Main articles: [Culper Ring](/wiki/Culper_Ring "Culper Ring") and [Military career of Benedict Arnold, 1777\'961779](/wiki/Military_career_of_Benedict_Arnold,_1777%E2%80%931779 "Military career of Benedict Arnold, 1777\'961779")\
\
Washington became America's first spymaster by designing an espionage system against the British.[\\[156\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTENagy2016274-163) In 1778, Major [Benjamin Tallmadge](/wiki/Benjamin_Tallmadge "Benjamin Tallmadge") formed the [Culper Ring](/wiki/Culper_Ring "Culper Ring") at Washington's direction to covertly collect information about the British in New York.[\\[157\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTERose200675,_224,_258\'96261-164) Washington had disregarded incidents of disloyalty by [Benedict Arnold](/wiki/Benedict_Arnold "Benedict Arnold"), who had distinguished himself in many campaigns, including the [Invasion of Quebec](/wiki/Invasion_of_Quebec_(1775) "Invasion of Quebec (1775)") and the [Battle of Saratoga](/wiki/Battle_of_Saratoga "Battle of Saratoga").[\\[158\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010378\'96387Philbrick201635-165)\
\
In 1780, Arnold began supplying British spymaster [John Andr\'e9](/wiki/John_Andr%C3%A9 "John Andr\'e9") with sensitive information intended to compromise Washington and capture [West Point](/wiki/West_Point,_New_York "West Point, New York"), a key American defensive position on the [Hudson River](/wiki/Hudson_River "Hudson River").[\\[159\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdams1928365\'96366Philbrick2016250\'96251-166) Historians [Nathaniel Philbrick](/wiki/Nathaniel_Philbrick "Nathaniel Philbrick") and [Ron Chernow](/wiki/Ron_Chernow "Ron Chernow") noted possible reasons for Arnold's defection to be his anger at losing promotions to junior officers, or repeated slights from Congress. He was also deeply in debt, profiteering from the war, and disappointed by Washington's lack of support during his eventual [court-martial](/wiki/Court-martial "Court-martial").[\\[160\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010380Palmer2010203Flexner1991119\'96221Rose2006196-167)\
\
After repeated requests, Washington agreed to give Arnold command of West Point in August.[\\[161\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010378,_380\'96381Lengel2005322Adams1928366Philbrick2016280\'96282-168) On September 21, Arnold met Andr\'e9 and gave him plans to take over the garrison.[\\[162\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdams1928365Palmer2010306,_315,_319,_320-169) While returning to British lines, Andr\'e9 was captured by militia who discovered the plans; upon hearing the news of Andr\'e9's capture on September 24, while waiting to greet and have breakfast with Washington, Arnold immediately fleed to the [HMS _Vulture_](/wiki/HMS_Vulture_(1776) "HMS Vulture (1776)"), the ship that had brought Andr\'e9 to West Point, and escaped to New York.[\\[163\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVan_Doren1941194\'96195Adams1928366Palmer2010410-170)\
\
Upon being told about Arnold's treason, Washington recalled the commanders positioned under Arnold at key points around the fort to prevent any complicity. He assumed personal command at West Point and reorganized its defenses.[\\[164\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPalmer2010370\'96371Middlekauff2015232-171) Andr\'e9's trial for espionage ended in a death sentence, and Washington offered to return him to the British in exchange for Arnold, but [Clinton](/wiki/Henry_Clinton_(British_Army_officer,_born_1730) "Henry Clinton (British Army officer, born 1730)") refused. Andr\'e9 was hanged on October 2, 1780, despite his request for a firing squad, to deter other spies.[\\[165\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlexner1991386Rose2006212-172)\
\
### Southern theater and Yorktown\
\
Main article: [Southern theater of the American Revolutionary War](/wiki/Southern_theater_of_the_American_Revolutionary_War "Southern theater of the American Revolutionary War")\
\
[![Generals Washington and Rochambeau, standing in front of HQ tent, giving last orders before the attack on Yorktown](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Couder_Yorktown_Versailles.JPG/260px-Couder_Yorktown_Versailles.JPG)](/wiki/File:Couder_Yorktown_Versailles.JPG)\
\
Generals Washington and [Rochambeau](/wiki/Jean-Baptiste_Donatien_de_Vimeur,_comte_de_Rochambeau "Jean-Baptiste Donatien de Vimeur, comte de Rochambeau") give final orders before launching the [Siege of Yorktown](/wiki/Siege_of_Yorktown "Siege of Yorktown") in [Yorktown, Virginia](/wiki/Yorktown,_Virginia "Yorktown, Virginia") in September 1781.\
\
In late 1778, General Clinton shipped 3,000 troops from New York to Georgia and launched a Southern invasion against [Savannah](/wiki/Savannah,_Georgia "Savannah, Georgia"), reinforced by 2,000 British and Loyalist troops. They repelled an attack by American patriots and [French naval forces](/wiki/French_Navy "French Navy"), which bolstered the British war effort.[\\[166\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor2016230-173)\
\
In June 1778, [Iroquois](/wiki/Iroquois "Iroquois") warriors joined with [Loyalist](/wiki/Loyalist_(American_Revolution) "Loyalist (American Revolution)") rangers led by [Walter Butler](/wiki/Walter_Butler_(Loyalist) "Walter Butler (Loyalist)") and killed more than 200 frontiersmen, laying waste to the [Wyoming Valley](/wiki/Wyoming_Valley "Wyoming Valley") in [Northeastern Pennsylvania](/wiki/Northeastern_Pennsylvania "Northeastern Pennsylvania").[\\[167\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlden1996184-174) In mid-1779, in response to this and other attacks on New England towns, Washington ordered General John Sullivan to lead an expedition to force the Iroquois out of New York by effecting "the total destruction and devastation" of their villages and taking their women and children hostage.[\\[168\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrizzard2002303-175)[\\[169\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010360-176) The expedition systematically destroyed Iroquois villages and food stocks, and forced at least 5,036 Iroquois to flee to British Canada. The campaign directly killed a few hundred Iroquois, but according to historian Rhiannon Koehler, the net effect was to reduce the Iroquois by half. They became unable to survive the harsh winter of 1779\'961780; some historians now described the campaign as a genocide.[\\[170\\]](#cite_note-177)\
\
Washington's troops went into quarters at [Morristown, New Jersey](/wiki/Morristown,_New_Jersey "Morristown, New Jersey") for their worst winter of the war, with temperatures well below freezing. [New York Harbor](/wiki/New_York_Harbor "New York Harbor") was frozen, snow covered the ground for weeks, and the troops again lacked provisions.[\\[171\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMann2008108-178)\
\
In January 1780, Clinton assembled 12,500 troops and attacked [Charles Town, South Carolina](/wiki/Siege_of_Charleston "Siege of Charleston"), defeating General [Benjamin Lincoln](/wiki/Benjamin_Lincoln "Benjamin Lincoln"). By June, they occupied the South Carolina [Piedmont](/wiki/Piedmont_(United_States) "Piedmont (United States)").[\\[172\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor2016234-179) Clinton returned to New York and left 8,000 troops under the command of General [Charles Cornwallis](/wiki/Charles_Cornwallis,_1st_Marquess_Cornwallis "Charles Cornwallis, 1st Marquess Cornwallis").[\\[173\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor2016234\'96235-180) Congress replaced Lincoln with Horatio Gates; after his defeat in the [Battle of Camden](/wiki/Battle_of_Camden "Battle of Camden"), Gates was replaced by Nathanael Greene, Washington's initial choice, but the British had firm control of the South. Washington was reinvigorated, however, when Lafayette returned from France with more ships, men, and supplies,[\\[174\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlden1996187\'96188-181) and 5,000 veteran French troops led by Marshal [Rochambeau](/wiki/Jean-Baptiste_Donatien_de_Vimeur,_comte_de_Rochambeau "Jean-Baptiste Donatien de Vimeur, comte de Rochambeau") arrived at [Newport, Rhode Island](/wiki/Newport,_Rhode_Island "Newport, Rhode Island") in July 1780.[\\[175\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTELancasterPlumb1985311-182) French naval forces then landed, led by Admiral [de Grasse](/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Joseph_Paul_de_Grasse "Fran\'e7ois Joseph Paul de Grasse").[\\[176\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlden1996197\'96199,_206-183)\
\
Washington's army went into winter quarters at [New Windsor, New York](/wiki/New_Windsor,_New_York "New Windsor, New York") in December 1780; he urged Congress and state officials to expedite provisions so the army would not "continue to struggle under the same difficulties they have hitherto endured".[\\[177\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlden1996193-184) On March 1, 1781, Congress ratified the [Articles of Confederation](/wiki/Articles_of_Confederation "Articles of Confederation"), but the government that took effect on March 2 did not have the power to levy taxes, and it loosely held the states together.[\\[178\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor2016339-185)\
\
General Clinton sent Benedict Arnold, now a British Brigadier General with 1,700 troops, to Virginia to capture [Portsmouth](/wiki/Portsmouth,_Virginia "Portsmouth, Virginia") and conduct raids on Patriot forces; Washington responded by sending Lafayette south to counter Arnold's efforts.[\\[179\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010403-186) Washington initially hoped to bring the fight to New York, drawing off British forces from Virginia and ending the war there, but Rochambeau advised him that Cornwallis in Virginia was the better target. De Grasse's fleet arrived off the Virginia coast, cutting off British retreat. Seeing the advantage, Washington made a feint towards Clinton in New York, then headed south to Virginia.[\\[180\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlden1996198\'96199Chernow2010403\'96404-187)\
\
#### Yorktown\
\
Main article: [Siege of Yorktown](/wiki/Siege_of_Yorktown "Siege of Yorktown")\
\
The siege of Yorktown was a decisive victory by the combined forces of the Continental Army commanded by Washington, the French Army commanded by General Comte de Rochambeau, and the French Navy commanded by Admiral de Grasse. On August 19, the march to Yorktown led by Washington and Rochambeau began, which is known now as the "[celebrated march](/wiki/Washington%E2%80%93Rochambeau_Revolutionary_Route "Washington\'96Rochambeau Revolutionary Route")".[\\[181\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTELengel2005335-188) Washington was in command of an army of 7,800 Frenchmen, 3,100 militia, and 8,000 Continentals. Inexperienced in siege warfare, he often deferred to the judgment of General Rochambeau and relied on his advice. Despite this, Rochambeau never challenged Washington's authority as the battle's commanding officer.[\\[182\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010413-189)\
\
By late September, Patriot-French forces surrounded Yorktown, trapped the British Army, and prevented British reinforcements from Clinton in the North, while the French navy emerged victorious at the [Battle of the Chesapeake](/wiki/Battle_of_the_Chesapeake "Battle of the Chesapeake"). The final American offensive began with a shot fired by Washington.[\\[183\\]](#cite_note-190) The siege ended with a British surrender on October 19, 1781; over 7,000 British soldiers became [prisoners of war](/wiki/Prisoner_of_war "Prisoner of war").[\\[184\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlden1996198,_201Chernow2010372\'96373,_418Lengel2005337-191) Washington negotiated the terms of surrender for two days, and the official signing ceremony took place on October 19; Cornwallis claimed illness and was absent, sending General [Charles O'Hara](/wiki/Charles_O%27Hara "Charles O'Hara") as his proxy.
