[\\[185\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMann200838LancasterPlumb1985254Chernow2010419-192) As a gesture of goodwill, Washington held a dinner for the American, French, and British generals, all of whom fraternized on friendly terms and identified with one another as members of the same professional military [caste](/wiki/Caste "Caste").[\\[186\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010419-193)\
\
Afterwards, Washington moved the army to [New Windsor, New York](/wiki/New_Windsor,_New_York "New Windsor, New York") where they remained stationed until the [Treaty of Paris](/wiki/Treaty_of_Paris_(1783) "Treaty of Paris (1783)") was signed on September 3, 1783, formally ending the war. Although the peace treaty did not happen for two years following the end of the battle, Yorktown proved to be the last significant battle or campaign of the Revolutionary War, with the British Parliament agreeing to cease hostilities in March 1782.[\\[187\\]](#cite_note-194)\
\
### Demobilization and resignation\
\
Main article: [George Washington's resignation as commander-in-chief](/wiki/George_Washington%27s_resignation_as_commander-in-chief "George Washington's resignation as commander-in-chief")\
\
[![Painting by John Trumbull, depicting General Washington, standing in Maryland State House hall, surrounded by statesmen and others, resigning his commission](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/General_George_Washington_Resigning_his_Commission.jpg/260px-General_George_Washington_Resigning_his_Commission.jpg)](/wiki/File:General_George_Washington_Resigning_his_Commission.jpg)\
\
_[General George Washington Resigning His Commission](/wiki/General_George_Washington_Resigning_His_Commission "General George Washington Resigning His Commission")_, an 1824 portrait by [John Trumbull](/wiki/John_Trumbull "John Trumbull")\
\
When peace negotiations began in April 1782, both the British and French began gradually evacuating their forces.[\\[188\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor2016313\'96315-195) With the American treasury empty, unpaid and mutinous soldiers forced the adjournment of Congress. In March 1783, Washington successfully calmed the [Newburgh Conspiracy](/wiki/Newburgh_Conspiracy "Newburgh Conspiracy"), a planned munity by American officers; Congress promised each a five-year bonus.[\\[189\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKohn1970187\'96220-196) Washington submitted an account of $450,000 in expenses which he had advanced to the army, equivalent to $9.15\'a0million in 2022. The account was settled, though it was allegedly vague about large sums and included expenses his wife had incurred through visits to his headquarters.[\\[190\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlden1996209-197)\
\
The following month, a Congressional committee led by [Alexander Hamilton](/wiki/Alexander_Hamilton "Alexander Hamilton") began adapting the army for peacetime. In August 1783, Washington gave the Army's perspective to the committee in his _Sentiments on a Peace Establishment_, which advised Congress to keep a standing army, create a "national militia" of separate state units, and establish a navy and a national military academy.[\\[191\\]](#cite_note-198)\
\
The [Treaty of Paris](/wiki/Treaty_of_Paris_(1783) "Treaty of Paris (1783)") was signed on September 3, 1783, and Britain officially recognized American independence. Washington disbanded his army, giving a farewell address to his soldiers on November 2.[\\[192\\]](#cite_note-199) During this time, Washington oversaw the [evacuation of British forces in New York](/wiki/Evacuation_Day_(New_York) "Evacuation Day (New York)") and was greeted by parades and celebrations.[\\[193\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010446,_448\'96449,_451Puls2008184\'96186-200) Along with Governor [George Clinton](/wiki/George_Clinton_(vice_president) "George Clinton (vice president)"), he took formal possession of the city on November 25.[\\[194\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTERandall1997405-201)\
\
In early December 1783, Washington bade farewell to his officers at [Fraunces Tavern](/wiki/Fraunces_Tavern "Fraunces Tavern") and [resigned as commander-in-chief](/wiki/George_Washington%27s_resignation_as_commander-in-chief "George Washington's resignation as commander-in-chief") soon thereafter.[\\[195\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor2016319-202) In a final appearance in uniform, he gave a statement to the Congress: "I consider it an indispensable duty to close this last solemn act of my official life, by commending the interests of our dearest country to the protection of Almighty God, and those who have the superintendence of them, to his holy keeping."[\\[196\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlden1996210Chernow2010451\'96452,_455-203) Washington's resignation was acclaimed at home and abroad and showed a skeptical world that the new republic would not degenerate into chaos.[\\[h\\]](#cite_note-205)[\\[198\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010454\'96455-206)\
\
The same month, Washington was appointed president-general of the [Society of the Cincinnati](/wiki/Society_of_the_Cincinnati "Society of the Cincinnati"), a newly established hereditary fraternity of Revolutionary War officers. He served in this capacity for the remainder of his life.[\\[i\\]](#cite_note-208)[\\[200\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010444-209)\
\
Early republic (1783\'961789)\
--------------------------\
\
Further information: [Confederation Period](/wiki/Confederation_Period "Confederation Period") and [Articles of Confederation](/wiki/Articles_of_Confederation "Articles of Confederation")\
\
### Return to Mount Vernon\
\
.mw-parser-output .quotebox\{background-color:#F9F9F9;border:1px solid #aaa;box-sizing:border-box;padding:10px;font-size:88%;max-width:100%\}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft\{margin:.5em 1.4em .8em 0\}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright\{margin:.5em 0 .8em 1.4em\}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.centered\{overflow:hidden;position:relative;margin:.5em auto .8em auto\}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft span,.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright span\{font-style:inherit\}.mw-parser-output .quotebox>blockquote\{margin:0;padding:0;border-left:0;font-family:inherit;font-size:inherit\}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-title\{background-color:#F9F9F9;text-align:center;font-size:110%;font-weight:bold\}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote>:first-child\{margin-top:0\}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote:last-child>:last-child\{margin-bottom:0\}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:before\{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" \'93 ";vertical-align:-45%;line-height:0\}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:after\{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" \'94 ";line-height:0\}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .left-aligned\{text-align:left\}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .right-aligned\{text-align:right\}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .center-aligned\{text-align:center\}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .quote-title,.mw-parser-output .quotebox .quotebox-quote\{display:block\}.mw-parser-output .quotebox cite\{display:block;font-style:normal\}@media screen and (max-width:640px)\{.mw-parser-output .quotebox\{width:100%!important;margin:0 0 .8em!important;float:none!important\}\}\
\
> "I am not only retired from all public employments but I am retiring within myself, and shall be able to view the solitary walk and tread the paths of private life with heartfelt satisfaction\'a0... I will move gently down the stream of life, until I sleep with my fathers."\
\
\'97 George Washington in a letter to Lafayette.  \
February 1, 1784.[\\[201\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTERandall1997410Flexner1974182\'96183DalzellDalzell1998112-210)\
\
Washington was longing to return home after spending just ten days at Mount Vernon out of .mw-parser-output .frac\{white-space:nowrap\}.mw-parser-output .frac .num,.mw-parser-output .frac .den\{font-size:80%;line-height:0;vertical-align:super\}.mw-parser-output .frac .den\{vertical-align:sub\}.mw-parser-output .sr-only\{border:0;clip:rect(0,0,0,0);clip-path:polygon(0px 0px,0px 0px,0px 0px);height:1px;margin:-1px;overflow:hidden;padding:0;position:absolute;width:1px\}8+1\uc0\u8260 2 years of war. He arrived on Christmas Eve, delighted to be "free of the bustle of a camp and the busy scenes of public life".[\\[202\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling2009246-211) He was a celebrity and was [f\'eated](https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/feted "wiktionary:feted") during a visit to his mother at Fredericksburg in February 1784, and he received a constant stream of visitors wishing to pay their respects at Mount Vernon.[\\[203\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010462Ferling2009255\'96256-212)\
\
Washington reactivated his interests in the [Great Dismal Swamp](/wiki/Great_Dismal_Swamp "Great Dismal Swamp") and [Potomac canal](/wiki/Potomac_Company "Potomac Company") projects begun before the war, though neither paid him any dividends, and he undertook a 34-day, 680-mile (1,090\'a0km) trip to check on his land holdings in the Ohio Country.[\\[204\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling2009247\'96255-213) He oversaw the completion of the remodeling work at Mount Vernon, which transformed his residence into the mansion that survives to this day\'97although his financial situation was not strong. Creditors paid him in depreciated wartime currency, and he owed significant amounts in taxes and wages. Mount Vernon had made no profit during his absence, and he saw persistently poor crop yields due to pestilence and poor weather. His estate recorded its eleventh year running at a deficit in 1787, and there was little prospect of improvement.[\\[205\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling2009246\'96247Chernow2010552\'96553Ellis2004167-214)\
\
To make his estate profitable again, Washington undertook a new landscaping plan and succeeded in cultivating a range of fast-growing trees and native shrubs.[\\[206\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWulf201252Subak201843\'9644-215) He also began breeding [mules](/wiki/Mule "Mule") after being gifted a [Spanish jack](/wiki/Andalusian_donkey "Andalusian donkey") by [King Charles III of Spain](/wiki/King_Charles_III_of_Spain "King Charles III of Spain") in 1784. There were few mules in the United States at that time, and he believed that they would revolutionize [agriculture](/wiki/Agriculture "Agriculture") and [transportation](/wiki/Transportation "Transportation").[\\[207\\]](#cite_note-216)\
\
### Constitutional Convention of 1787\
\
Main article: [Constitutional Convention (United States)](/wiki/Constitutional_Convention_(United_States) "Constitutional Convention (United States)")\
\
[![Painting by Howard Chandler Christy, depicting the signing of the Constitution of the United States, with Washington as the presiding officer standing at right](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Scene_at_the_Signing_of_the_Constitution_of_the_United_States.jpg/260px-Scene_at_the_Signing_of_the_Constitution_of_the_United_States.jpg)](/wiki/File:Scene_at_the_Signing_of_the_Constitution_of_the_United_States.jpg)\
\
_[Scene at the Signing of the Constitution of the United States](/wiki/Scene_at_the_Signing_of_the_Constitution_of_the_United_States "Scene at the Signing of the Constitution of the United States")_, a 1940 portrait by [Howard Chandler Christy](/wiki/Howard_Chandler_Christy "Howard Chandler Christy") depicting Washington as the presiding officer at the [Constitutional Convention](/wiki/Constitutional_Convention_(United_States) "Constitutional Convention (United States)") in 1787\
\
Before returning to private life in June 1783, Washington called for a strong union. Though he was concerned that he might be criticized for meddling in civil matters, he sent a circular letter to the states, maintaining that the [Articles of Confederation](/wiki/Articles_of_Confederation "Articles of Confederation") was no more than "a rope of sand". He believed the nation was on the verge of "anarchy and confusion", was vulnerable to foreign intervention, and that a national constitution would unify the states under a strong central government.[\\[208\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlden1996221Chernow2010518Ferling2009266-217)\
\
When [Shays' Rebellion](/wiki/Shays%27_Rebellion "Shays' Rebellion") erupted in Massachusetts over taxation, Washington was further convinced that a national constitution was needed.[\\[209\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010517\'96519-218) Some nationalists feared that the new republic had descended into lawlessness, and they met on September 11, 1786, at [Annapolis](/wiki/Annapolis_Convention_(1786) "Annapolis Convention (1786)") to ask Congress to revise the Articles of Confederation. One of their biggest efforts was getting Washington to attend.[\\[210\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor2016373\'96374Ferling2009266-219) Congress agreed to a Constitutional Convention to be held in Philadelphia in Spring 1787, with each state to send delegates.[\\[211\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010523Taylor2016373\'96374-220)\
\
On December 4, 1786, Washington was chosen to lead the Virginia delegation, but he declined on December 21. He had concerns about the legality of the convention and consulted [James Madison](/wiki/James_Madison "James Madison"), [Henry Knox](/wiki/Henry_Knox "Henry Knox"), and others. They persuaded him to attend as his presence might induce reluctant states to send delegates and smooth the way for the ratification process while also giving legitimacy to the convention.[\\[212\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010220\'96221Ferling2009266-221) On March 28, Washington told Governor [Edmund Randolph](/wiki/Edmund_Randolph "Edmund Randolph") that he would attend the convention but made it clear that he was urged to attend.[\\[213\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling2009266Chernow2010218,_220\'96224,_520\'96526-222)\
\
Washington arrived in Philadelphia on May 9, 1787, though a quorum was not attained until May 25. Benjamin Franklin nominated Washington to preside over the convention, and he was unanimously elected to serve as president general.[\\[214\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010520\'96521,_523,_526,_529Unger201333-223) The convention's state-mandated purpose was to revise the Articles of Confederation, and the new government would be established when the resulting document was "duly confirmed by the several states".[\\[215\\]](#cite_note-224) Randolph introduced Madison's [Virginia Plan](/wiki/Virginia_Plan "Virginia Plan") on May 27, the third day of the convention. It called for an entirely new constitution and a sovereign national government, which Washington highly recommended.[\\[216\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling1988359\'96360-225)\
\
On July 10, Washington wrote to [Alexander Hamilton](/wiki/Alexander_Hamilton "Alexander Hamilton"): "I almost despair of seeing a favorable issue to the proceedings of our convention and do therefore repent having had any agency in the business."[\\[217\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlden1996226\'96227-226) Nevertheless, he lent his prestige to the work of the other delegates, unsuccessfully lobbying many to support ratification of the [Constitution](/wiki/Constitution_of_the_United_States "Constitution of the United States"), such as anti-federalists [Edmund Randolph](/wiki/Edmund_Randolph "Edmund Randolph") and [George Mason](/wiki/George_Mason "George Mason").[\\[218\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlden1996229-227) The final version was voted on and [signed by 39 of 55 delegates](/wiki/Signing_of_the_United_States_Constitution "Signing of the United States Constitution") on September 17, 1787.\
\
### Chancellor of William & Mary\
\
In 1788, the Board of Visitors of the [College of William & Mary](/wiki/College_of_William_%26_Mary "College of William & Mary") decided to re-establish the position of Chancellor, and elected Washington to the office on January 18.[\\[219\\]](#cite_note-chancellor-228) The College Rector Samuel Griffin wrote to Washington inviting him to the post, and in a letter dated April 30, 1788, Washington accepted the position of the 14th [Chancellor of the College of William & Mary](/wiki/Chancellor_of_the_College_of_William_%26_Mary "Chancellor of the College of William & Mary").[\\[220\\]](#cite_note-229) He continued to serve through his presidency until his death on December 14, 1799.[\\[219\\]](#cite_note-chancellor-228)\
\
### First presidential election\
\
Main article: [1788\'961789 United States presidential election](/wiki/1788%E2%80%931789_United_States_presidential_election "1788\'961789 United States presidential election")\
\
The delegates to the Convention anticipated a Washington presidency and left it to him to define the office once elected.[\\[j\\]](#cite_note-231)[\\[217\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlden1996226\'96227-226)\
\
The state electors under the Constitution voted for the president on February 4, 1789, and Washington suspected that most republicans had not voted for him.[\\[222\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010559\'96560Ferling2009361-232) The mandated March\'a04 date passed without a Congressional quorum to count the votes, but a quorum was reached on April 5. The votes were tallied the next day, and Washington won the majority of every state's electoral votes. He was informed of his election as president by Congressional Secretary [Charles Thomson](/wiki/Charles_Thomson "Charles Thomson").[\\[223\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010551-233) John Adams received the next highest number of votes and was elected vice president.[\\[224\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling2009274-234) Despite feeling "anxious and painful sensations" about leaving Mount Vernon, he departed for New York City on April 16 to be inaugurated.[\\[225\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling2009274\'96275Chernow2010559\'96561-235)[\\[226\\]](#cite_note-236)\
\
Presidency (1789\'961797)\
----------------------\
\
Main article: [Presidency of George Washington](/wiki/Presidency_of_George_Washington "Presidency of George Washington")\
\
[![Painting by Gilbert Stuart (1795), formal portrait of President George Washington](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/George_Washington_1795.jpg/220px-George_Washington_1795.jpg)](/wiki/File:George_Washington_1795.jpg)\
\
_President George Washington_, a 1795 portrait by [Gilbert Stuart](/wiki/Gilbert_Stuart "Gilbert Stuart")\
\
Washington was [inaugurated](/wiki/Presidency_of_George_Washington "Presidency of George Washington") on April 30, 1789, taking the [oath of office](/wiki/Oath_of_office_of_the_President_of_the_United_States "Oath of office of the President of the United States") at [Federal Hall](/wiki/Federal_Hall "Federal Hall") in New York City.[\\[k\\]](#cite_note-238)[\\[228\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooke20024Chernow2010550\'96551Fitzpatrick1936522-239) His coach was led by militia and a marching band and followed by statesmen and foreign dignitaries in an inaugural parade, with a crowd of 10,000.[\\[229\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010566\'96567Randall1997448-240) Chancellor [Robert R. Livingston](/wiki/Robert_R._Livingston_(chancellor) "Robert R. Livingston (chancellor)") administered the oath, using a [Bible provided by the Masons](/wiki/George_Washington_Inaugural_Bible "George Washington Inaugural Bible"), after which the militia fired a 13-gun salute.[\\[230\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooke20024Chernow2010568-241) Washington read a speech in the Senate Chamber, asking "that Almighty Being ... consecrate the liberties and happiness of the people of the United States".[\\[231\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTERandall1997448Alden1996236-242) Though he wished to serve without a salary, Congress insisted that he accept it, later providing Washington $25,000 per year to defray costs of the presidency, equivalent to $6.14\'a0million today.[\\[232\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010552Fitzpatrick1936v._19,_p._522-243) Washington wrote to [James Madison](/wiki/James_Madison "James Madison"): "As the first of everything in our situation will serve to establish a precedent, it is devoutly wished on my part that these precedents be fixed on true principles."[\\[233\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUnger201376-244) To that end, he preferred the title "Mr. President" over more majestic names proposed by the Senate, including "His Excellency" and "His Highness the President".[\\[234\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBassett1906155-245) His executive precedents included the inaugural address, messages to Congress, and the [cabinet form](/wiki/Cabinet_of_the_United_States "Cabinet of the United States") of the [executive branch](/wiki/Federal_government_of_the_United_States "Federal government of the United States").[\\[235\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUnger2013236\'96237-246)\
\
Washington planned to resign after his first term, but political strife convinced him to remain in office.[\\[236\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010674\'96675-247) He was an able administrator and a judge of talent and character, and he regularly talked with department heads to get their advice.[\\[237\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllis2004197\'96198Unger2013236\'96237-248) He tolerated opposing views, despite fears that a democratic system would lead to political violence, and he conducted a smooth transition of power to his successor.[\\[238\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGenovese2009589Unger2013236\'96237-249) He remained non-partisan throughout his presidency and opposed the divisiveness of political parties, but he favored a strong central government, was sympathetic to a Federalist form of government, and leery of the Republican opposition.[\\[239\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010696\'96698Randall1997478-250)\
\
Washington dealt with major problems. The old [Confederation](/wiki/Confederation_Period "Confederation Period") lacked the powers to handle its workload and had weak leadership, no executive, a small bureaucracy of clerks, large debt, worthless paper money, and no power to establish taxes.[\\[240\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooke20025-251) He had the task of assembling an executive department and relied on [Tobias Lear](/wiki/Tobias_Lear "Tobias Lear") for advice selecting its officers.[\\[241\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010575-252) Britain refused to relinquish its forts in the American West,[\\[240\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooke20025-251) and [Barbary pirates](/wiki/Barbary_pirates#18th\'9619th_centuries "Barbary pirates") preyed on American merchant ships in the Mediterranean before the United States even had a navy.[\\[242\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010514-253)\
\
### Cabinet and executive departments\
\
See also: [Cabinet of the United States](/wiki/Cabinet_of_the_United_States "Cabinet of the United States")\
\

1795\'961797\
\
Congress created executive departments in 1789, including the [State Department](/wiki/United_States_Department_of_State "United States Department of State") in July, the [War Department](/wiki/United_States_Department_of_War "United States Department of War") in August, and the [Treasury Department](/wiki/United_States_Department_of_the_Treasury "United States Department of the Treasury") in September. Washington appointed [Edmund Randolph](/wiki/Edmund_Randolph "Edmund Randolph") as Attorney General, [Samuel Osgood](/wiki/Samuel_Osgood "Samuel Osgood") as Postmaster General, [Thomas Jefferson](/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson "Thomas Jefferson") as [Secretary of State](/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_State "United States Secretary of State"), [Henry Knox](/wiki/Henry_Knox "Henry Knox") as [Secretary of War](/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_War "United States Secretary of War"), and [Alexander Hamilton](/wiki/Alexander_Hamilton "Alexander Hamilton") as [Secretary of the Treasury](/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_the_Treasury "United States Secretary of the Treasury"). Washington's cabinet became a consulting and advisory body, not mandated by the Constitution.[\\[243\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling2009281\'96282Cooke20024\'965-254)\
\
Washington's cabinet members formed rival parties with sharply opposing views, most fiercely illustrated between Hamilton and Jefferson.[\\[244\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooke20025Banning19745-255) Washington restricted cabinet discussions to topics of his choosing, without participating in the debate. He occasionally requested cabinet opinions in writing and expected department heads to agreeably carry out his decisions.[\\[240\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooke20025-251)\
\
### Domestic issues\
\
Washington was apolitical and opposed the formation of parties, suspecting that conflict would undermine republicanism.[\\[245\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEElkinsMcKitrick1995290-256) He exercised great restraint in using [his veto power](/wiki/List_of_United_States_presidential_vetoes "List of United States presidential vetoes"), writing that "I give my Signature to many Bills with which my Judgment is at variance..."[\\[246\\]](#cite_note-257)\
\
His closest advisors formed two factions, portending the [First Party System](/wiki/First_Party_System "First Party System"). Secretary of the Treasury [Alexander Hamilton](/wiki/Alexander_Hamilton "Alexander Hamilton") formed the [Federalist Party](/wiki/Federalist_Party "Federalist Party") to promote national credit and a financially powerful nation. Secretary of State [Thomas Jefferson](/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson "Thomas Jefferson") opposed Hamilton's agenda and founded the [Jeffersonian Republicans](/wiki/Democratic-Republican_Party "Democratic-Republican Party"). Washington favored Hamilton's agenda, however, and it ultimately went into effect\'97resulting in bitter controversy.[\\[247\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooke20027-258)\
\
Washington proclaimed November 26, 1789, as a day of [Thanksgiving](/wiki/Thanksgiving_(United_States) "Thanksgiving (United States)") to encourage national unity. "It is the duty of all nations to acknowledge the providence of Almighty God, to obey His will, to be grateful for His benefits, and humbly to implore His protection and favor." He spent that day fasting and visiting debtors in prison to provide them with food and beer.[\\[248\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010585,_609Henriques200665NovakNovak2007144\'96146-259)\
\
#### African Americans\
\
In response to two antislavery petitions that were presented to Congress in 1790, slaveholders in Georgia and South Carolina threatened to "blow the trumpet of civil war". Washington and Congress responded with a series of racist measures: [naturalization was denied](/wiki/Naturalization_Act_of_1790 "Naturalization Act of 1790") to black immigrants; [blacks were barred from serving](/wiki/Militia_Acts_of_1792 "Militia Acts of 1792") in state militias; the [Southwest Territory](/wiki/Southwest_Territory "Southwest Territory") (later the state of Tennessee) was permitted to maintain slavery; and two more slave states were admitted (Kentucky in 1792 and Tennessee in 1796). On February 12, 1793, Washington signed into law the [Fugitive Slave Act](/wiki/Fugitive_Slave_Act_of_1793 "Fugitive Slave Act of 1793"), which overrode state laws and courts, allowing agents to cross state lines to return escaped slaves.[\\[249\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010758Taylor2016399\'96400-260) Many free blacks in the north decried the law believing it would allow bounty hunting and kidnapping. The Fugitive Slave Act gave effect to the Constitution's [Fugitive Slave Clause](/wiki/Fugitive_Slave_Clause "Fugitive Slave Clause"), and the Act was passed overwhelmingly in Congress.[\\[250\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor2016399\'96400-261)\
\
At the same time, Washington signed a reenactment of the [Northwest Ordinance](/wiki/Northwest_Ordinance "Northwest Ordinance") in 1789, which had freed all slaves brought after 1787 into a vast expanse of federal territory north of the [Ohio River](/wiki/Ohio_River "Ohio River"), except for slaves escaping from slave states.[\\[251\\]](#cite_note-262)[\\[252\\]](#cite_note-263) The 1787 law lapsed when the new U.S. Constitution was ratified in 1789.[\\[253\\]](#cite_note-264) He also signed the [Slave Trade Act of 1794](/wiki/Slave_Trade_Act_of_1794 "Slave Trade Act of 1794"), which sharply limited American involvement in the [Atlantic slave trade](/wiki/Atlantic_slave_trade "Atlantic slave trade").[\\[254\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBassett1906187\'96189-265) On February 18, 1791, Congress admitted the free state of Vermont into the Union as the 14th state as of March 4, 1791.[\\[255\\]](#cite_note-266)\
\
#### National Bank\
\
[![Engraving of President Washington's House in Philadelphia, his residence from 1790 to 1797](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Philadelphia_Presidents_house.jpg/220px-Philadelphia_Presidents_house.jpg)](/wiki/File:Philadelphia_Presidents_house.jpg)\
\
[President's House](/wiki/President%27s_House_(Philadelphia) "President's House (Philadelphia)") in [Philadelphia](/wiki/Philadelphia "Philadelphia"), where both Washington and then [John Adams](/wiki/John_Adams "John Adams") worked and resided until completion of the [White House](/wiki/White_House "White House") in 1800\
\
Washington's first term was largely devoted to economic concerns.[\\[256\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2005345-267) Establishment of public credit became a primary challenge for the federal government.[\\[257\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBanning19745\'967-268) Hamilton submitted a report to a deadlocked Congress, and he, Madison, and Jefferson reached the [Compromise of 1790](/wiki/Compromise_of_1790 "Compromise of 1790") in which Jefferson agreed to Hamilton's debt proposals in exchange for moving the nation's capital temporarily to Philadelphia and then south near [Georgetown](/wiki/Georgetown_(Washington,_D.C.) "Georgetown (Washington, D.C.)") on the [Potomac River](/wiki/Potomac_River "Potomac River").[\\[247\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooke20027-258) The terms were legislated in the [Funding Act of 1790](/wiki/Funding_Act_of_1790 "Funding Act of 1790") and the [Residence Act](/wiki/Residence_Act "Residence Act"), both of which Washington signed into law. Congress authorized the assumption and payment of the nation's debts, with funding provided by customs duties and excise taxes.[\\[258\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooke20027\'968-269)\
\
Hamilton caused controversy in Cabinet by advocating for the establishment of the [First Bank of the United States](/wiki/First_Bank_of_the_United_States "First Bank of the United States"). Madison and Jefferson objected to the idea, but legislation creating the bank easily passed Congress. Jefferson and [Randolph](/wiki/Edmund_Randolph "Edmund Randolph") insisted the federal government was going beyond its constitutional authority. Hamilton argued the government could charter the bank under the implied powers granted by the constitution. Washington sided with Hamilton and signed the bank legislation on February 25, 1791. The rift between Hamilton and Jefferson, meanwhile, became openly hostile.[\\[259\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooke20028-270)\
\
The nation's [first financial crisis](/wiki/Panic_of_1792 "Panic of 1792") occurred in March 1792. Hamilton's Federalists exploited large loans to gain control of U.S. debt securities, causing a run on the national bank;[\\[260\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTESobel196827-271) the markets returned to normal by mid-April.[\\[261\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBanning19749Sobel196830-272) Jefferson believed Hamilton was part of the scheme, despite Hamilton's efforts to ameliorate.[\\[262\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010673\'96674-273)\
\
#### Jefferson\'96Hamilton feud\
\
[![Formal portrait of Thomas Jefferson, part of a dual image of Jefferson and Hamilton](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/T_Jefferson_by_Charles_Willson_Peale_1791_2.jpg/144px-T_Jefferson_by_Charles_Willson_Peale_1791_2.jpg)](/wiki/File:T_Jefferson_by_Charles_Willson_Peale_1791_2.jpg)\
\
[![Formal portrait of Alexander Hamilton, part of a dual image of Jefferson and Hamilton](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Alexander_Hamilton_portrait_by_John_Trumbull_1806%2C_detail.jpg/170px-Alexander_Hamilton_portrait_by_John_Trumbull_1806%2C_detail.jpg)](/wiki/File:Alexander_Hamilton_portrait_by_John_Trumbull_1806,_detail.jpg)\
\
[Thomas Jefferson](/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson "Thomas Jefferson") _(left)_ and [Alexander Hamilton](/wiki/Alexander_Hamilton "Alexander Hamilton") _(right)_ held opposing political views and frequently clashed in cabinet meetings, much to Washington's dismay.\
\
Jefferson and Hamilton adopted diametrically opposed political principles. Hamilton believed in a strong national government requiring a national bank and foreign loans to function, while Jefferson believed the states and the farm element should primarily direct the government; he also resented the idea of banks and foreign loans. To Washington's dismay, the two men persistently entered into disputes and infighting.[\\[263\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010515,_627\'96630,_648\'96650Randall1997452,_463,_468\'96471-274) Hamilton demanded that Jefferson resign if he could not support Washington, and Jefferson told Washington that Hamilton's fiscal system would lead to the overthrow of the republic.[\\[264\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBanning19748Cooke20029-275) Washington urged them to call a truce for the sake of the nation, but they ignored him.[\\[265\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooke20029Fitzpatrick1936v._19,_p._523-276)\
\
Jefferson's political actions, his support of Freneau's _National Gazette_,[\\[266\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEElkinsMcKitrick1995240,_285,_290,_361-277) and his attempts to undermine Hamilton nearly led Washington to dismiss him from the cabinet; he ultimately resigned his position in December 1793, and Washington forsook him.[\\[267\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooke20029Chernow2005427-278)\
\
The feud led to the well-defined Federalist and Republican parties, and party affiliation became necessary for election to Congress by 1794.[\\[268\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling2013222,_283\'96284,_301\'96302-279) Washington remained aloof from congressional attacks on Hamilton, but did not publicly protect him. The [Hamilton\'96Reynolds sex scandal](/wiki/Hamilton%E2%80%93Reynolds_affair "Hamilton\'96Reynolds affair") opened Hamilton to disgrace, but Washington continued to hold him in "very high esteem".[\\[269\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling2013301\'96302-280)\
\
#### Whiskey Rebellion\
\
Main article: [Whiskey Rebellion](/wiki/Whiskey_Rebellion "Whiskey Rebellion")\
\
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/WhiskeyRebellion.jpg/220px-WhiskeyRebellion.jpg)](/wiki/File:WhiskeyRebellion.jpg)\
\
Washington reviews the troops near [Fort Cumberland, Maryland](/wiki/Fort_Cumberland_(Maryland) "Fort Cumberland (Maryland)"), before their march to suppress the [Whiskey Rebellion](/wiki/Whiskey_Rebellion "Whiskey Rebellion") in [Western Pennsylvania](/wiki/Western_Pennsylvania "Western Pennsylvania")\
\
In March 1791, at Hamilton's urging, with support from Madison, Congress imposed an excise tax on distilled spirits to help curtail the national debt, which took effect in July.[\\[270\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2005342\'96343-281) Grain farmers strongly protested in Pennsylvania's frontier districts; they argued that they were unrepresented and were shouldering too much of the debt, comparing their situation to British taxation pre-Revolution.\
\
On August 2, Washington assembled his cabinet to discuss the situation. Unlike Washington, who had reservations about using force, Hamilton was eager to suppress the rebellion with federal authority.[\\[271\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKohn1972567\'96568,_570-282) Wanting to avoid involving the federal government, Washington first called on [Pennsylvania](/wiki/Pennsylvania "Pennsylvania") state officials to take the initiative, but they declined. On August 7, Washington issued his first proclamation for calling up state militias. After appealing for peace, he reminded the protestors that, unlike the rule of the British crown, the Federal law was issued by state-elected representatives.[\\[272\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010719\'96721Puls2008219-283)\
\
Threats and violence against tax collectors, however, escalated into defiance against federal authority in 1794 and gave rise to the [Whiskey Rebellion](/wiki/Whiskey_Rebellion "Whiskey Rebellion"). Washington issued a final proclamation on September 25, threatening the use of military force to no avail.[\\[272\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010719\'96721Puls2008219-283) The federal army was not up to the task, so Washington invoked the [Militia Act of 1792](/wiki/Militia_Acts_of_1792 "Militia Acts of 1792") to summon state militias.[\\[273\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoakley199643\'9649-284) Governors sent troops, initially commanded by Washington, who handed over command to [Henry Lee](/wiki/Henry_Lee_III "Henry Lee III") to lead them into the rebellious districts. They took 150 prisoners, and the remaining rebels dispersed. Two of the prisoners were condemned to death, but Washington exercised his Constitutional authority for the first time and pardoned them.[\\[274\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010721,_726Kohn1972567\'96584-285)\
\
Washington's forceful action demonstrated that the new government could protect itself and its tax collectors. This represented the first use of federal military force against the states and citizens.[\\[275\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKohn1972567\'96584-286) Washington justified his action against "certain self-created societies", which he regarded as "subversive organizations" that threatened the national union. He did not dispute their right to protest, but he insisted that their dissent must not violate federal law. Congress agreed and extended their congratulations to him; only Madison and Jefferson expressed indifference.[\\[276\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllis2004225\'96226-287)\
\
### Foreign affairs\
\
In April 1792, the [French Revolutionary Wars](/wiki/French_Revolutionary_Wars "French Revolutionary Wars") began between Britain and France, and Washington declared America's neutrality. The revolutionary government of France sent diplomat [Edmond-Charles Gen\'eat](/wiki/Edmond-Charles_Gen%C3%AAt "Edmond-Charles Gen\'eat") to America, and he was welcomed with great enthusiasm. He created a network of new [Democratic-Republican Societies](/wiki/Democratic-Republican_Societies "Democratic-Republican Societies") promoting France's interests, but Washington denounced them and demanded that the French recall Gen\'eat.[\\[277\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEElkinsMcKitrick1995335\'96354-288) The National Assembly of France granted Washington honorary French citizenship on August 26, 1792, during the early stages of the [French Revolution](/wiki/French_Revolution "French Revolution").[\\[278\\]](#cite_note-289)\
\
Hamilton formulated the [Jay Treaty](/wiki/Jay_Treaty "Jay Treaty") to normalize trade relations with Britain while removing them from western forts, and also to resolve financial debts remaining from the Revolution.[\\[279\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEElkinsMcKitrick1995ch._9-290) Chief Justice [John Jay](/wiki/John_Jay "John Jay") acted as Washington's negotiator and signed the treaty on November 19, 1794; critical Jeffersonians, however, supported France. Washington deliberated, then supported the treaty because it avoided war with Britain,[\\[280\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010730-291) but was disappointed that its provisions favored Britain.[\\[281\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling2009340-292) He mobilized public opinion and secured ratification in the Senate[\\[282\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEstes2000393\'96422Estes2001127\'96158-293) but faced frequent public criticism.[\\[283\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling2009344-294)\
\
The British agreed to abandon their forts around the [Great Lakes](/wiki/Great_Lakes "Great Lakes"), and the United States modified the boundary with Canada. The government liquidated numerous pre-Revolution debts, and the British opened the [British West Indies](/wiki/British_West_Indies "British West Indies") to American trade. The treaty secured peace with Britain and a decade of prosperous trade. Jefferson claimed that it angered France and "invited rather than avoided" war.[\\[284\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling2009343-295) Relations with France deteriorated afterward and, two days before Washington's term ended, the French Directory declared the authority to seize American ships,[\\[285\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAkers200227-296) leaving succeeding president [John Adams](/wiki/John_Adams "John Adams") with prospective war.[\\[286\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrizzard2005263Lengel2005357-297)\
\
### Native American affairs\
\
Main articles: [Northwest Indian War](/wiki/Northwest_Indian_War "Northwest Indian War"), [Treaty of New York (1790)](/wiki/Treaty_of_New_York_(1790) "Treaty of New York (1790)"), and [Treaty of Greenville](/wiki/Treaty_of_Greenville "Treaty of Greenville")\
\
Further information: [Northwest Territory](/wiki/Northwest_Territory "Northwest Territory") and [Ohio Country](/wiki/Ohio_Country "Ohio Country")\
\
[![A R.F. Zogbaum scene of the Battle of Fallen Timbers includes Native Americans aiming as cavalry soldiers charge with raised swords and one soldier is shot and loses his mount](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Fallen_timbers.jpg/220px-Fallen_timbers.jpg)](/wiki/File:Fallen_timbers.jpg)\
\
_Battle of Fallen Timbers_, an 1896 portrait by [Rufus Fairchild Zogbaum](/wiki/Rufus_Fairchild_Zogbaum "Rufus Fairchild Zogbaum") depicting the final battle of the [Northwest Indian War](/wiki/Northwest_Indian_War "Northwest Indian War")\
\
During the fall of 1789, Washington had to contend with the British refusing to evacuate their forts in the Northwest frontier and their [concerted efforts](/wiki/Northwest_Indian_War "Northwest Indian War") to incite Indian tribes to attack American settlers.[\\[287\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFitzpatrick1936523Cooke20029\'9610Chernow2010665-298) The Northwest tribes under [Miami](/wiki/Miami_people "Miami people") chief [Little Turtle](/wiki/Little_Turtle "Little Turtle") allied with the British to resist American expansion, and killed 1,500 settlers between 1783 and 1790.[\\[288\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaldmanBraun2009149-299)\
\
[![Portrait of Seneca Chief Sagoyewatha, Washington's peace emissary](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Red_Jacket_2.jpg/180px-Red_Jacket_2.jpg)](/wiki/File:Red_Jacket_2.jpg)\
\
[Seneca](/wiki/Seneca_people "Seneca people") chief [Red Jacket](/wiki/Red_Jacket "Red Jacket") was Washington's peace emissary with the [Northwestern Confederacy](/wiki/Northwestern_Confederacy "Northwestern Confederacy")\
\
Washington declared that "the Government of the United States are determined that their Administration of Indian Affairs shall be directed entirely by the great principles of Justice and humanity", and provided that treaties should negotiate their land interests.[\\[289\\]](#cite_note-300) The administration regarded powerful tribes as foreign nations, and Washington even smoked a [peace pipe](/wiki/Ceremonial_pipe "Ceremonial pipe") and drank wine with them at the [President's House](/wiki/President%27s_House_(Philadelphia) "President's House (Philadelphia)") in Philadelphia.[\\[290\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalloway20182-301) He made numerous attempts to conciliate them;[\\[291\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlexner1969304Taylor2016406-302) he equated killing indigenous peoples with killing whites and sought to integrate them into [European American](/wiki/European_Americans "European Americans") culture.[\\[292\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooke200210-303)\
\
In the Southwest, negotiations failed between federal commissioners and raiding Indian tribes seeking retribution. Washington invited [Creek](/wiki/Muscogee "Muscogee") Chief [Alexander McGillivray](/wiki/Alexander_McGillivray "Alexander McGillivray") and 24 leading chiefs to New York to negotiate a treaty and treated them like foreign dignitaries. Knox and McGillivray concluded the [Treaty of New York](/wiki/Treaty_of_New_York_(1790) "Treaty of New York (1790)") on August 7, 1790, which provided the tribes with agricultural supplies and McGillivray with the rank of Brigadier General and an annual salary of $1,200, equivalent to $28,404 in 2022.[\\[293\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrizzard2002256\'96257Puls2008207\'96208-304)\
\
In 1790, Washington sent Brigadier General [Josiah Harmar](/wiki/Josiah_Harmar "Josiah Harmar") to pacify the Northwest tribes, but Little Turtle routed him twice and forced him to withdraw.[\\[294\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010667\'96678Gaff2004xviiWaldmanBraun2009149-305) The [Northwestern Confederacy](/wiki/Northwestern_Confederacy "Northwestern Confederacy") of tribes used guerrilla tactics and were an effective force against the sparsely manned American Army. Washington sent Major General [Arthur St. Clair](/wiki/Arthur_St._Clair "Arthur St. Clair") from [Fort Washington](/wiki/Fort_Washington_(Ohio) "Fort Washington (Ohio)") on an expedition to restore peace in the territory in 1791. On November 4, St. Clair's forces were ambushed and [soundly defeated](/wiki/St._Clair%27s_defeat "St. Clair's defeat") by tribal forces with few survivors.[\\[295\\]](#cite_note-306)\
\
Washington replaced the disgraced St. Clair with the Revolutionary War hero [Anthony Wayne](/wiki/Anthony_Wayne "Anthony Wayne"). From 1792 to 1793, Wayne instructed his troops on Native American warfare tactics and instilled discipline which was lacking under St. Clair.[\\[296\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooke200210Chernow2010668-307) In August 1794, Washington sent Wayne into tribal territory with authority to drive them out by burning their villages and crops in the [Maumee Valley](/wiki/Maumee,_Ohio "Maumee, Ohio").[\\[297\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor2016406Chernow2010668-308) On August 24, the American army defeated the Northwestern Confederacy at the [Battle of Fallen Timbers](/wiki/Battle_of_Fallen_Timbers "Battle of Fallen Timbers"), and the [Treaty of Greenville](/wiki/Treaty_of_Greenville "Treaty of Greenville") in August 1795 opened two-thirds of the [Ohio Country](/wiki/Ohio_Country "Ohio Country") for American settlement.[\\[298\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooke200214Taylor2016406-309)\
\
### Second term\
\
[![Painting of the frigate USS Constitution with three masts](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Constitution1803.jpg/220px-Constitution1803.jpg)](/wiki/File:Constitution1803.jpg)\
\
Portrait of the [USS _Constitution_](/wiki/USS_Constitution "USS Constitution"), commissioned and named by President Washington in 1794\
\
Washington initially planned to retire after his first term, weary of office and in poor health. After dealing with the infighting in his own cabinet and with partisan critics, he showed little enthusiasm for a second term, while Martha also wanted him not to run.[\\[299\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010674\'96675,_678Ferling2009362Randall1997484-310) Washington's nephew George Augustine Washington, managing Mount Vernon in his absence, was critically ill, further increasing Washington's desire to retire.[\\[300\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010687-311)\
\
Many, however, urged him to run for a second term. Madison told him that his absence would only allow the dangerous political rift in his cabinet and the House to worsen. Jefferson also pleaded with him not to retire, agreeing to drop his attacks on Hamilton, and stating that he would also retire if Washington did.[\\[301\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling1988421Randall1997482Chernow2010675,_678-312) Hamilton maintained that Washington's absence would be "deplored as the greatest evil" to the country.[\\[302\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2005403-313) With the [election of 1792](/wiki/1792_United_States_presidential_election "1792 United States presidential election") nearing, Washington relented and agreed to run.[\\[292\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooke200210-303)\
\
On February 13, 1793, the [Electoral College](/wiki/United_States_Electoral_College "United States Electoral College") unanimously re-elected Washington president, and [John Adams](/wiki/John_Adams "John Adams") as vice president by a vote of 77 to 50.[\\[292\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooke200210-303) He was sworn into office by Associate Justice [William Cushing](/wiki/William_Cushing "William Cushing") on March 4, 1793, in the Senate Chamber of [Congress Hall](/wiki/Congress_Hall "Congress Hall") in Philadelphia. Afterwards, Washington gave a brief address before immediately retiring to the [President's House](/wiki/President%27s_House_(Philadelphia) "President's House (Philadelphia)").[\\[303\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010687Cooke200210\'9611-314)\
\
On April 22, 1793, when the [French Revolutionary Wars](/wiki/French_Revolutionary_Wars "French Revolutionary Wars") broke out, Washington [issued a proclamation](/wiki/Proclamation_of_Neutrality "Proclamation of Neutrality") which declared American neutrality. He was resolved to pursue "a conduct friendly and impartial toward the belligerent Powers" while also warning Americans not to intervene in the conflict.[\\[304\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling2009299,_304,_308\'96311Banning19742Cooke200211\'9612-315) Although Washington recognized France's revolutionary government, he would eventually ask French minister to the United States [Edmond-Charles Gen\'eat](/wiki/Edmond-Charles_Gen%C3%AAt "Edmond-Charles Gen\'eat") be recalled over the Citizen Gen\'eat affair.[\\[305\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooke200212\'9613-316) Gen\'eat was a diplomatic troublemaker who was openly hostile toward Washington's neutrality policy. He procured four American ships as privateers to strike at Spanish forces (British allies) in [Florida](/wiki/Florida "Florida") while organizing militias to strike at other [British possessions](/wiki/British_possessions "British possessions"). However, his efforts failed to draw the United States into the conflict.[\\[306\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010692Cooke200212-317)\
\
On July 31, 1793, Jefferson submitted his resignation from cabinet.[\\[307\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooke200213-318) Hamilton, desiring more income for his family, resigned from office in January 1795 and was replaced by [Oliver Wolcott Jr.](/wiki/Oliver_Wolcott_Jr. "Oliver Wolcott Jr."). While his relationship with Washington would remain friendly, Washington's relationship with his Secretary of War Henry Knox deteriorated after rumors that Knox had profited from contracts for the construction of U.S. frigates which had been commissioned under the [Naval Act of 1794](/wiki/Naval_Act_of_1794 "Naval Act of 1794") in order to combat [Barbary pirates](/wiki/Barbary_pirates "Barbary pirates"), forcing Knox to resign.[\\[308\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010713-319)[\\[309\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010726\'96727Cooke200215-320)\
\
In the final months of his presidency, Washington was assailed by his political foes and a partisan press who accused him of being ambitious and greedy. He came to regard the press as a disuniting, "diabolical" force of falsehoods.[\\[310\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTERandall1997491\'96492Chernow2010752\'96754-321) At the end of his second term, Washington retired for personal and political reasons, dismayed with personal attacks, and to ensure that a truly contested presidential election could be held. He did not feel bound to a two-term limit, but his retirement set a significant precedent.[\\[311\\]](#cite_note-322)\
\
### Farewell Address\
\
Main article: [George Washington's Farewell Address](/wiki/George_Washington%27s_Farewell_Address "George Washington's Farewell Address")\
\
[![Newspaper showing Washington's Farewell Address](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Washington%27s_Farewell_Address.jpg/220px-Washington%27s_Farewell_Address.jpg)](/wiki/File:Washington%27s_Farewell_Address.jpg)\
\
[Washington's Farewell Address](/wiki/George_Washington%27s_Farewell_Address "George Washington's Farewell Address"), published by the _[American Daily Advertiser](/wiki/Pennsylvania_Packet "Pennsylvania Packet")_ on September 19, 1796\
\
In 1796, Washington declined to run for a third term of office.[\\[312\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpaldingGarrity199658Lurie2018-323) In May 1792, in anticipation of his retirement, Washington instructed [James Madison](/wiki/James_Madison "James Madison") to prepare a "[valedictory address](/wiki/Valedictorian "Valedictorian")", an initial draft of which was entitled the "Farewell Address".[\\[313\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpaldingGarrity199646\'9647-324) In May 1796, Washington sent the manuscript to [Alexander Hamilton](/wiki/Alexander_Hamilton "Alexander Hamilton") who did an extensive rewrite, while Washington provided final edits.[\\[314\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlexner1972292Chernow2010752\'96753SpaldingGarrity19964744Hayes2017287\'96298-325) On September 19, 1796, David Claypoole's _[American Daily Advertiser](/wiki/Pennsylvania_Packet "Pennsylvania Packet")_ published the final version.[\\[315\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010754Lurie2018-326)\
\
Washington stressed that national identity was paramount, as a united America would safeguard freedom and prosperity. He warned the nation of three eminent dangers: regionalism, partisanship, and foreign entanglements, and said the "name of AMERICAN, which belongs to you, in your national capacity, must always exalt the just pride of patriotism".[\\[316\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010755Lurie2018-327) Washington called for men to move beyond partisanship for the common good, stressing that the United States must concentrate on its own interests. He warned against foreign alliances and their influence in domestic affairs, and bitter partisanship and the dangers of political parties.[\\[317\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTERandall1997492Boller196347-328) He counseled friendship and commerce with all nations, but advised against involvement in European wars.[\\[318\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFishmanPedersonRozell2001119\'96120GreggSpalding1999199\'96216-329) He stressed the importance of religion, asserting that "religion and morality are indispensable supports" in a republic.[\\[319\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010133-330) Washington's address favored Hamilton's Federalist ideology and economic policies.[\\[320\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTERandall1997492Cooke200218\'9619Flexner1972292\'96297Avlon2017223Boller196347-331)\
\
He closed the address by reflecting on his legacy:\
\
.mw-parser-output .templatequote\{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px\}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite\{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0\}\
\
> Though in reviewing the incidents of my Administration I am unconscious of intentional error, I am nevertheless too sensible of my defects not to think it probable that I may have committed many errors. Whatever they may be, I fervently beseech the Almighty to avert or mitigate the evils to which they may tend. I shall also carry with me the hope that my country will never cease to view them with indulgence, and that, after forty-five years of my life dedicated to its service with an upright zeal, the faults of incompetent abilities will be consigned to oblivion, as myself must soon be to the [mansions of rest](/wiki/John_14 "John 14").[\\[321\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAvlon2017280-332)\
\
After initial publication, many Republicans, including Madison, criticized the Address and described it as an anti-French campaign document, with Madison believing that Washington was strongly pro-British.[\\[322\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpaldingGarrity1996143-333)\
\
In 1839, Washington biographer [Jared Sparks](/wiki/Jared_Sparks "Jared Sparks") maintained that Washington's "Farewell Address was [printed and published](/wiki/Early_American_publishers_and_printers "Early American publishers and printers") with the laws, by order of the legislatures, as an evidence of the value they attached to its political precepts, and of their affection for its author."[\\[323\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTESparks1839444-334) In 1972, Washington scholar [James Flexner](/wiki/James_Thomas_Flexner "James Thomas Flexner") referred to the Farewell Address as receiving as much acclaim as Thomas Jefferson's [Declaration of Independence](/wiki/United_States_Declaration_of_Independence "United States Declaration of Independence") and [Abraham Lincoln](/wiki/Abraham_Lincoln "Abraham Lincoln")'s [Gettysburg Address](/wiki/Gettysburg_Address "Gettysburg Address").[\\[324\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlexner1972292SpaldingGarrity1996142-335) In 2010, historian [Ron Chernow](/wiki/Ron_Chernow "Ron Chernow") called the _Farewell Address_ one of the most influential statements on republicanism.[\\[325\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010752\'96754-336)\
\
Post-presidency (1797\'961799)\
---------------------------\
\
Further information: [Post-presidency of George Washington](/wiki/Post-presidency_of_George_Washington "Post-presidency of George Washington")\
\
### Retirement\
\
Washington retired to Mount Vernon in March 1797 and devoted time to his plantations and other business interests.[\\[326\\]](#cite_note-337) His plantation operations were only minimally profitable,[\\[31\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow201053-34) and his lands in the west ([Piedmont](/wiki/Piedmont_(United_States) "Piedmont (United States)")) were under Indian attacks and yielded little income, with squatters there refusing to pay rent. He attempted to sell these but without success.[\\[327\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllis2004255\'96261-338) He became an even more committed Federalist. He vocally supported the [Alien and Sedition Acts](/wiki/Alien_and_Sedition_Acts "Alien and Sedition Acts") and convinced Federalist [John Marshall](/wiki/John_Marshall "John Marshall") to run for Congress to weaken the Jeffersonian hold on [Virginia](/wiki/Virginia "Virginia").[\\[328\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlexner1974386-339)\
\
Washington grew restless in retirement, prompted by tensions with France; in a continuation of the [French Revolutionary Wars](/wiki/French_Revolutionary_Wars "French Revolutionary Wars"), French privateers began seizing American ships in 1798, and relations deteriorated with France and led to the "[Quasi-War](/wiki/Quasi-War "Quasi-War")". Washington wrote to Secretary of War [James McHenry](/wiki/James_McHenry "James McHenry") offering to organize President Adams' army.[\\[329\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTERandall1997497-340) Adams nominated him for a lieutenant general commission on July 4, 1798, and the position of commander-in-chief of the armies.[\\[330\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlexner1974376\'96377Bell199264-341) Washington served as the commanding general from July 13, 1798, until his death 17 months later.[\\[331\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBell199264-342) He participated in planning for a provisional army, but avoided involvement in details. In advising McHenry of potential officers for the army, he appeared to make a complete break with Jefferson's Democratic-Republicans: "you could as soon scrub the blackamoor white, as to change the principles of a profest Democrat; and that he will leave nothing unattempted to overturn the government of this country."[\\[332\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFitzpatrick1936474vol._36-343) Washington delegated the active leadership of the army to Hamilton, a major general. No army invaded the United States during this period, and Washington did not assume a field command.[\\[333\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKohn1975225\'96242Grizzard2005264-344)\
\
Washington was known to be rich because of the well-known "glorified fa\'e7ade of wealth and grandeur" at Mount Vernon,[\\[334\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010708-345) but nearly all his wealth was in the form of land and slaves rather than ready cash. To supplement his income, he erected a [distillery](/wiki/File:George_Washington%27s_Distillery.JPG "File:George Washington's Distillery.JPG") for substantial whiskey production.[\\[335\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHirschfeld199744\'9645Ferling2009351-346) He bought land parcels to spur development around the new [Federal City](/wiki/Washington,_D.C. "Washington, D.C.") named in his honor, and he sold individual lots to middle-income investors rather than multiple lots to large investors, believing they would more likely commit to making improvements.[\\[336\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010704\'96705-347)\
\
### Final days and death\
\
[![Washington on his deathbed, with doctors and family surrounding](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Life_of_George_Washington%2C_Deathbed.jpg/260px-Life_of_George_Washington%2C_Deathbed.jpg)](/wiki/File:Life_of_George_Washington,_Deathbed.jpg)\
\
_Washington on his Deathbed_, an 1851 portrait by [Junius Brutus Stearns](/wiki/Junius_Brutus_Stearns "Junius Brutus Stearns")\
\
On December 12, 1799, Washington inspected his farms on horseback. He returned home late and had guests for dinner. He had a sore throat the next day but was well enough to mark trees for cutting. That evening, Washington complained of chest congestion.[\\[337\\]](#cite_note-348) The next morning, however, he awoke to an inflamed throat and difficulty breathing. He ordered estate overseer George Rawlins to remove nearly a pint of his blood; [bloodletting](/wiki/Bloodletting "Bloodletting") was a common practice of the time. His family summoned doctors [James Craik](/wiki/James_Craik "James Craik"), [Gustavus Richard Brown](/wiki/Gustavus_Richard_Brown "Gustavus Richard Brown"), and [Elisha C. Dick](/wiki/Elisha_C._Dick "Elisha C. Dick").[\\[338\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010806\'96810Morens19991845\'961849-349) A fourth doctor, [William Thornton](/wiki/William_Thornton "William Thornton"), arrived some hours after Washington died.[\\[339\\]](#cite_note-350)\
\
Brown initially believed Washington had [quinsy](/wiki/Peritonsillar_abscess "Peritonsillar abscess"); Dick thought the condition was a more serious "violent inflammation of the throat".[\\[340\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010806\'96807Lear1799257-351) They continued the process of bloodletting to approximately five pints, but Washington's condition deteriorated further. Dick proposed a [tracheotomy](/wiki/Tracheotomy "Tracheotomy"), but the other physicians were not familiar with that procedure and disapproved.[\\[341\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010806\'96810-352) Washington instructed Brown and Dick to leave the room, while he assured Craik, "Doctor, I die hard, but I am not afraid to go."[\\[342\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllis2004269-353)\
\
Washington's death came more swiftly than expected.[\\[343\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling2009365-354) On his deathbed, out of fear of being entombed alive, he instructed his private secretary [Tobias Lear](/wiki/Tobias_Lear "Tobias Lear") to wait three days before his burial.[\\[344\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010808-355) According to Lear, Washington died between 10 p.m. and 11 p.m. on December 14, 1799, with Martha seated at the foot of his bed. His last words were "'Tis well", from his conversation with Lear about his burial. He was 67.[\\[345\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlexner1974401\'96402Chernow2010808\'96809-356)\
\
Congress immediately adjourned for the day upon news of Washington's death, and the Speaker's chair was shroud in black the next morning.[\\[346\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIrving1857359-357) The funeral was held four days after his death on December 18, 1799, at Mount Vernon, where his body was interred. Cavalry and foot soldiers led the procession, and six colonels served as the pallbearers. The Mount Vernon funeral service was restricted mostly to family and friends.[\\[347\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010808\'96810-358) Reverend Thomas Davis read the funeral service by the vault with a brief address, followed by a ceremony performed by members of Washington's Masonic lodge in [Alexandria, Virginia](/wiki/Alexandria,_Virginia "Alexandria, Virginia").[\\[348\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIrving1857374\'96375-359) Word of his death traveled slowly; church bells rang in the cities, and many businesses closed.[\\[349\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010810\'96811-360) Memorial processions were held in major cities of the United States. Martha wore a black mourning cape for one year, and she burned their correspondence to protect their privacy. Only five letters between the couple are known to have survived: two from Martha to George and three from him to her.[\\[350\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010814-361)\
\
The diagnosis of Washington's illness and the immediate cause of his death have been subjects of debate since his death. The published account of doctors Craik and Brown stated that his symptoms were consistent with cynanche trachealis, a term then used to describe severe inflammation of the upper windpipe, including quinsy.[\\[l\\]](#cite_note-363) Accusations have persisted since Washington's death concerning medical malpractice.[\\[341\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010806\'96810-352) Modern medical authors have concluded that he likely died from severe [epiglottitis](/wiki/Epiglottitis "Epiglottitis") complicated by the treatments, including multiple doses of [calomel](/wiki/Mercury(I)_chloride "Mercury(I) chloride"), a purgative, and extensive bloodletting which almost certainly caused [hypovolemic shock](/wiki/Hypovolemia "Hypovolemia").[\\[m\\]](#cite_note-368)\
\
Burial, net worth, and aftermath\
--------------------------------\
\
See also: [Finances of George Washington](/wiki/Finances_of_George_Washington "Finances of George Washington")\
\
[![A picture of the two sarcophagi of George (at right) and Martha Washington at the present tomb at Mount Vernon](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Tomb_of_George_Washington_-_interior_02_-_Mount_Vernon.jpg/220px-Tomb_of_George_Washington_-_interior_02_-_Mount_Vernon.jpg)](/wiki/File:Tomb_of_George_Washington_-_interior_02_-_Mount_Vernon.jpg)\
\
The [sarcophagi](/wiki/Sarcophagus "Sarcophagus") of George (right) and [Martha Washington](/wiki/Martha_Washington "Martha Washington") at the entrance to their tomb in [Mount Vernon](/wiki/Mount_Vernon "Mount Vernon")\
\
Washington was buried in the old Washington family vault at Mount Vernon. At the time of his death, his estate was worth an estimated $780,000 in 1799, equivalent to $13.72\'a0million in 2022.[\\[356\\]](#cite_note-369) Washington's peak net worth was $587\'a0million, including 300 slaves.[\\[357\\]](#cite_note-370) Washington held title to more than 65,000 acres of land in 37 different locations.[\\[72\\]](#cite_note-Lehrman_Institute_(June_22,_2021)-78)\
\
In 1830, a disgruntled ex-employee of the estate [attempted to steal](/wiki/Attempted_theft_of_George_Washington%27s_skull "Attempted theft of George Washington's skull") what he thought was Washington's skull, prompting the construction of a more secure vault.[\\[358\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTECraughwell200977\'9679-371) In his will, Washington had left instructions for the construction of a new vault as the old family vault was crumbling and needed repair even before his death.[\\[349\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010810\'96811-360) A new vault was constructed at Mount Vernon the following year to receive the remains of George and Martha and other relatives.[\\[359\\]](#cite_note-NewTomb-372)\
\
In 1832, a joint Congressional committee debated moving his body from Mount Vernon to a crypt in the Capitol. The crypt had been built by architect [Charles Bulfinch](/wiki/Charles_Bulfinch "Charles Bulfinch") in the 1820s during the reconstruction of the burned-out capital, after the [Burning of Washington](/wiki/Burning_of_Washington "Burning of Washington") by the British during the [War of 1812](/wiki/War_of_1812 "War of 1812"). Southern opposition was intense, antagonized by an ever-growing rift between North and South; many were concerned that Washington's remains could end up on "a shore foreign to his native soil" if the country became divided, and Washington's remains stayed in Mount Vernon.[\\[360\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoorstin2010349\'96350-373)\
\
On October 7, 1837, Washington's remains, still in the original lead coffin, were placed within a marble sarcophagus designed by [William Strickland](/wiki/William_Strickland_(architect) "William Strickland (architect)") and constructed by John Struthers.[\\[361\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrickland184011\'9614Carlson2016chapter_1-374) The sarcophagus was sealed and encased with planks, and an outer vault was constructed around it.[\\[362\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrickland184011\'9614-375) The outer vault has the sarcophagi of both George and Martha Washington; the inner vault has the remains of other Washington family members and relatives.[\\[359\\]](#cite_note-NewTomb-372)\
\
Personal life\
-------------\
\
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Edward_Savage_-_The_Washington_Family_-_Google_Art_ProjectFXD.jpg/220px-Edward_Savage_-_The_Washington_Family_-_Google_Art_ProjectFXD.jpg)](/wiki/File:Edward_Savage_-_The_Washington_Family_-_Google_Art_ProjectFXD.jpg)\
\
_[The Washington Family](/wiki/The_Washington_Family "The Washington Family")_, a late 18th century portrait by [Edward Savage](/wiki/Edward_Savage_(artist) "Edward Savage (artist)") depicting George and [Martha Washington](/wiki/Martha_Washington "Martha Washington") with her grandchildren and an unnamed enslaved man\
\
Washington was somewhat reserved in personality, but was known for having a strong presence. He made speeches and announcements when required, but he was not a noted orator or debater.[\\[363\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling200216Randall199734,_436Chernow201029\'9630-376) He was taller than most of his contemporaries;[\\[364\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling200216-377) accounts of his height vary from 6\'a0ft (1.83\'a0m) to 6\'a0ft 3.5\'a0in (1.92\'a0m) tall,[\\[64\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010122-69)[\\[365\\]](#cite_note-378) he weighed between 210\'96220 pounds (95\'96100\'a0kg) as an adult,[\\[366\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling200216Chernow201029\'9630-379) and was known for his great strength.[\\[367\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010123\'96125-380)\
\
He had grey-blue eyes and long reddish-brown hair. He did not wear a [powdered wig](/wiki/Wig#19th_and_20th_centuries "Wig"); instead he wore his hair curled, powdered, and tied in a queue in the [fashion of the day](/wiki/1775%E2%80%931795_in_Western_fashion#Men's_fashion "1775\'961795 in Western fashion").[\\[368\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow201030-381)[\\[369\\]](#cite_note-382)\
\
Washington frequently suffered from severe [tooth decay](/wiki/Tooth_decay "Tooth decay") and ultimately lost all [his teeth](/wiki/George_Washington%27s_teeth "George Washington's teeth") but one. He had several sets of false teeth during his presidency. Contrary to common lore, these were not made of wood, but of metal, ivory, bone, animal teeth, and human teeth possibly obtained from slaves.[\\[370\\]](#cite_note-383) These dental problems left him in constant pain, which he treated with [laudanum](/wiki/Laudanum "Laudanum").[\\[371\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow201030,_290,_437\'96439,_642\'96643-384)\
\
Washington was a talented equestrian, with [Thomas Jefferson](/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson "Thomas Jefferson") describing him as "the best horseman of his age".[\\[372\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010124-385) He collected thoroughbreds at Mount Vernon, his two favorite horses being [Blueskin](/wiki/Blueskin_(horse) "Blueskin (horse)") and [Nelson](/wiki/Nelson_(horse) "Nelson (horse)").[\\[373\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010124,_469-386) He enjoyed hunting foxes, deer, ducks, and other game.[\\[374\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010469-387) He was an excellent dancer and frequently attended the theater. He drank alcohol in moderation but was morally opposed to excessive drinking, smoking tobacco, gambling, and profanity.[\\[375\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010134-388)\
\
### Religion and Freemasonry\
\
Main articles: [Religious views of George Washington](/wiki/Religious_views_of_George_Washington "Religious views of George Washington") and [American Enlightenment](/wiki/American_Enlightenment "American Enlightenment")\
\
[![Washington is shown presiding as Master Mason over a lodge meeting.](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Washington_Masonic_print.jpg/220px-Washington_Masonic_print.jpg)](/wiki/File:Washington_Masonic_print.jpg)\
\
_Washington as Master of his Lodge_, an 1870 rendering of the 1793 event\
\
Washington was descended from [Anglican](/wiki/Anglicanism "Anglicanism") minister [Lawrence Washington](/wiki/Lawrence_Washington_(1602%E2%80%931652) "Lawrence Washington (1602\'961652)"), whose troubles with the [Church of England](/wiki/Church_of_England "Church of England") may have prompted his heirs to emigrate to America.[\\[376\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTETsakiridis2018-389) He was baptized as an infant in April 1732 and became a devoted member of the Anglican Church.[\\[377\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow20106Morrison2009136Alden19962,_26Randall199717Tsakiridis2018-390) He served more than 20 years as a [vestryman](/wiki/Vestryman "Vestryman") and churchwarden at [Fairfax Parish](/wiki/Fairfax_Parish,_Virginia "Fairfax Parish, Virginia") and [Truco Parish](/wiki/Truro_Parish,_Virginia "Truro Parish, Virginia") in Virginia.[\\[378\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010130Thompson200840Tsakiridis2018-391) He privately prayed and read the Bible daily, and publicly encouraged people and the nation to pray.[\\[379\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrazer2012198\'96199Chernow2010119,_132Tsakiridis2018-392) He may have taken communion on a regular basis prior to the Revolution, but he did not do so following the war.[\\[380\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010131,_470Johnstone191987\'96195Frazer2012201\'96203Tsakiridis2018-393)\
\
Washington believed in a "wise, inscrutable, and irresistible" Creator God who was active in the Universe, contrary to [deistic thought](/wiki/Deism "Deism").[\\[376\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTETsakiridis2018-389) He referred to God in [Enlightenment](/wiki/Enlightenment_(spiritual) "Enlightenment (spiritual)") terms, including _[Providence](/wiki/Divine_providence "Divine providence")_, the _Creator_, or the _Almighty_, and the _Divine Author_ or _Supreme Being_.[\\[381\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTERandall199767Tsakiridis2018-394) He believed in a divine power who watched over battlefields, was involved in the outcome of war, protected his life, and was involved in American politics and specifically the creation of the United States.[\\[n\\]](#cite_note-396)[\\[383\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010131Tsakiridis2018-397) Historian [Ron Chernow](/wiki/Ron_Chernow "Ron Chernow") has argued that Washington avoided evangelistic Christianity or hellfire-and-brimstone speech along with communion or anything inclined to "flaunt his religiosity", saying that he "never used his religion as a device for partisan purposes or in official undertakings".[\\[384\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010131\'96132-398) No mention of [Jesus Christ](/wiki/Jesus_Christ "Jesus Christ") appears in [his private correspondence](/wiki/The_Washington_Papers "The Washington Papers"), and such references are rare in his public writings.[\\[385\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTENovakNovak200795Tsakiridis2018-399) At the same time, Washington frequently quoted from the Bible or paraphrased it, and often referred to the Anglican _[Book of Common Prayer](/wiki/Book_of_Common_Prayer "Book of Common Prayer")_.[\\[386\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010131\'96132Morrison2009136Tsakiridis2018-400)\
\
Washington emphasized religious toleration in a nation with numerous denominations and religions. He publicly attended services of different Christian denominations and prohibited [anti-Catholic](/wiki/Anti-Catholicism_in_the_United_States#Origins "Anti-Catholicism in the United States") celebrations in the Army.[\\[387\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoller1963125-401) He engaged workers at Mount Vernon without regard for religious belief or affiliation. While president, he acknowledged major religious sects and gave speeches on religious toleration.[\\[388\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010131-402) He was distinctly rooted in the ideas, values, and modes of thinking of the Enlightenment,[\\[389\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWood2001313-403) but he harbored no contempt of organized Christianity and its clergy, "being no bigot myself to any mode of worship".[\\[389\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWood2001313-403) In 1793, speaking to members of the [New Church](/wiki/The_New_Church_(Swedenborgian) "The New Church (Swedenborgian)") in [Baltimore](/wiki/Baltimore "Baltimore"), Washington said, "We have abundant reason to rejoice that in this Land the light of truth and reason has triumphed over the power of bigotry and superstition."[\\[390\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTENovakNovak2007117,_n._52-404)\
\
[Freemasonry](/wiki/Freemasonry "Freemasonry") was a widely accepted institution in the late 18th century, known for advocating moral teachings.[\\[391\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010132,_500Morrison2009136Stavish2007XIX,_XXIImmekus2018-405) Washington was attracted to the Masons' dedication to the Enlightenment principles of rationality, reason, and brotherhood. American Masonic lodges did not share the anti-clerical views of the [controversial European lodges](/wiki/Anticlericalism_and_Freemasonry "Anticlericalism and Freemasonry").[\\[392\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow201027,_704-406)\
\
A Masonic lodge was established in [Fredericksburg, Virginia](/wiki/Fredericksburg,_Virginia "Fredericksburg, Virginia") in September 1752, and Washington was initiated two months later at the age of 20 as one of its first Entered Apprentices. Within a year, he progressed through its ranks to become a Master Mason.[\\[393\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTERandall199767Chernow201027-407) Washington had high regard for the Masonic Order, but his lodge attendance was sporadic. In 1777, a convention of Virginia lodges asked him to be the Grand Master of the newly established [Grand Lodge of Virginia](/wiki/Grand_Lodge_of_Virginia "Grand Lodge of Virginia"), but he declined due to his commitments leading the Continental Army. After 1782, he frequently corresponded with Masonic lodges and members,[\\[394\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEImmekus2018-408) and he was listed as Master in the Virginia charter of [Alexandria Lodge No. 22](/wiki/George_Washington_Masonic_National_Memorial "George Washington Masonic National Memorial") in 1788.[\\[395\\]](#cite_note-409)\
\
Slavery\
-------\
\
Main article: [George Washington and slavery](/wiki/George_Washington_and_slavery "George Washington and slavery")\
\
Further information: [Slavery in the colonial United States](/wiki/Slavery_in_the_colonial_United_States "Slavery in the colonial United States") and [Slavery in the United States](/wiki/Slavery_in_the_United_States "Slavery in the United States")\
\
In Washington's lifetime, slavery was [deeply ingrained](/wiki/History_of_slavery_in_Virginia "History of slavery in Virginia") in the economic and social fabric of the [Colony of Virginia](/wiki/Colony_of_Virginia "Colony of Virginia"), which continued after the Revolution and the establishment of [Virginia](/wiki/Virginia "Virginia") as a state.[\\[396\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHenriques2006146-410)[\\[397\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWillcoxArnstein198841\'9642-411) Slavery was [legal in all of the Thirteen Colonies](/wiki/Slavery_in_the_colonial_history_of_the_United_States "Slavery in the colonial history of the United States") prior to the American Revolution.[\\[398\\]](#cite_note-Maloy_(April_6,_2021)-412)\
\
### Washington's slaves\
\
[![Washington the farmer is shown standing on his plantation talking to an overseer as children play and slaves work.
