Work is by Junius Stearns.](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Junius_Brutus_Stearns_-_George_Washington_as_Farmer_at_Mount_Vernon.jpg/220px-Junius_Brutus_Stearns_-_George_Washington_as_Farmer_at_Mount_Vernon.jpg)](/wiki/File:Junius_Brutus_Stearns_-_George_Washington_as_Farmer_at_Mount_Vernon.jpg)\
\
_Washington the Farmer at Mount Vernon_, an 1851 portrait by [Junius Brutus Stearns](/wiki/Junius_Brutus_Stearns "Junius Brutus Stearns")\
\
Washington owned and rented enslaved [African Americans](/wiki/African_Americans "African Americans"), and during his lifetime over 577 slaves lived and worked at Mount Vernon.[\\[399\\]](#cite_note-Mount_Vernon_Slaves-413)[\\[400\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan2000279Ellis200445-414) He acquired them through inheritance, gaining control of 84 [dower](/wiki/Dowry "Dowry") slaves upon his marriage to Martha, and purchased at least 71 slaves between 1752 and 1773.[\\[401\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan2000279\'96280Morgan2005405,_407_n7Hirschfeld199712-415) From 1786, he rented slaves; at the time of his death he was renting 41.[\\[402\\]](#cite_note-416)[\\[399\\]](#cite_note-Mount_Vernon_Slaves-413)\
\
Prior to the Revolutionary War, Washington's view on slavery was the same as most Virginia [planters](/wiki/Planter_class "Planter class") of the time.[\\[403\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTETwohig2001116-417) Beginning in the 1760s, however, Washington gradually grew to oppose it. His first doubts were prompted by his transition from tobacco to grain crops, which left him with a costly surplus of slaves, causing him to question the system's economic efficiency.[\\[404\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan2005413-418) His growing disillusionment with the institution was spurred by the principles of the Revolution and revolutionary friends such as Lafayette and Hamilton.[\\[405\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTETwohig2001121Morgan2005426-419) Most historians agree the Revolution was central to the evolution of Washington's attitudes on slavery;[\\[406\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFurstenberg2011260-420) "After 1783," Kenneth Morgan writes, "... \\[Washington\\] began to express inner tensions about the problem of slavery more frequently, though always in private".[\\[407\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan2000299-421) Regardless, Washington would remain dependent on slave labor to work his farms.[\\[408\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTETwohig2001122\'96123Morgan2000283,_289-422)\
\
The many contemporary reports of slave treatment at Mount Vernon are varied and conflicting.[\\[409\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWiencek2003348-423) Historian Kenneth Morgan maintains that Washington was frugal on spending for clothes and bedding for his slaves, and only provided them with just enough food, and that he maintained strict control over his slaves, instructing his overseers to keep them working hard from dawn to dusk year-round.[\\[410\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan2000286\'96287-424) In contrast, historian Dorothy Twohig said: "Food, clothing, and housing seem to have been at least adequate".[\\[411\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTETwohig2001-425)\
\
Washington faced growing debts involved with the costs of supporting slaves. He held an "engrained sense of racial superiority" towards [African Americans](/wiki/African_Americans "African Americans") but harbored no ill feelings toward them.[\\[412\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan2000283,_285,_286-426) Some enslaved families worked at different locations on the plantation but were allowed to visit one another on their days off.[\\[413\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan2000282,_283\'96285Chernow2010112\'96113-427) Washington's slaves received two hours off for meals during the workday and were given time off on Sundays and religious holidays.[\\[414\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010111Ferling200246Schwarz200127,_83-428)[\\[415\\]](#cite_note-429)\
\
Some accounts report that Washington opposed flogging but at times sanctioned its use, generally as a last resort, on both men and women slaves.[\\[416\\]](#cite_note-430) Washington used both reward and punishment to encourage discipline and productivity in his slaves. He tried appealing to an individual's sense of pride, gave better blankets and clothing to the "most deserving", and motivated his slaves with cash rewards. He believed "watchfulness and admonition" were better deterrents against transgressions but would punish those who "will not do their duty by fair means". Punishment ranged in severity from demotion back to fieldwork, through whipping and beatings, to permanent separation from friends and family by sale. Historian Ron Chernow maintains that overseers were required to warn slaves before resorting to the lash and required Washington's written permission before whipping, though his extended absences did not always permit this.[\\[417\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010113\'96114-431)\
\
[![Runaway advertisement from the May 24, 1796, Pennsylvania Gazette, Philadelphia, Pennsylvania.](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Oney_Judge_Runaway_Ad.jpg/180px-Oney_Judge_Runaway_Ad.jpg)](/wiki/File:Oney_Judge_Runaway_Ad.jpg)\
\
Runaway advertisement for [Ona Judge](/wiki/Oney_Judge "Oney Judge"), enslaved servant in Washington's presidential household\
\
During his presidency, Washington brought several of his slaves to the federal capital. When the capital moved from New York City to Philadelphia in 1791, the president began rotating his slave household staff periodically between the capital and Mount Vernon. This was done deliberately to circumvent Pennsylvania's [Slavery Abolition Act](/wiki/An_Act_for_the_Gradual_Abolition_of_Slavery "An Act for the Gradual Abolition of Slavery"), which stated that any slave who lived there for more than six months was automatically freed.[\\[418\\]](#cite_note-432)\
\
In May 1796, Martha's personal and favorite slave [Ona Judge](/wiki/Oney_Judge "Oney Judge") escaped to [Portsmouth, New Hampshire](/wiki/Portsmouth,_New_Hampshire "Portsmouth, New Hampshire"). At Martha's behest, Washington attempted to capture Ona, using a Treasury agent, but failed. In February 1797, around the time of his 65th birthday, Washington's personal slave [Hercules Posey](/wiki/Hercules_Posey "Hercules Posey") escaped from Mount Vernon to Philadelphia and was never found.[\\[419\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010759\'96763-433)\
\
In February 1786, Washington took a census of Mount Vernon and recorded 224 slaves.[\\[420\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan2000279\'96287-434) By 1799, the slave population at Mount Vernon totaled 317, including 143 children.[\\[421\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan2000281\'96282-435) Washington owned 124 slaves, leased 40, and held 153 for his wife's dower interest.[\\[422\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan2000298-436)[\\[6\\]](#cite_note-Slavery-at-Mount-Vernon-7) Washington supported many slaves who were too young or too old to work, greatly increasing Mount Vernon's slave population and causing the plantation to operate at a loss.[\\[423\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWiencek2003319,_348\'96349Flexner1974386Hirschfeld19972Ellis2004167Morgan2000283-437)\
\
### Abolition and manumission\
\
Main article: [Abolitionism in the United States](/wiki/Abolitionism_in_the_United_States "Abolitionism in the United States")\
\
Based on his private papers and on accounts from his contemporaries, Washington slowly developed a cautious sympathy toward [abolitionism](/wiki/Abolitionism_in_the_United_States "Abolitionism in the United States") that eventually ended with his will [freeing](/wiki/Manumission "Manumission") his long-time valet [Billy Lee](/wiki/William_Lee_(valet) "William Lee (valet)"), and then subsequently freeing the rest of his personally owned slaves outright upon Martha's death.[\\[424\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHirschfeld19973Morgan200029-438)[\\[425\\]](#cite_note-439) As president, he remained publicly silent on the topic of slavery, believing it was a nationally divisive issue that could undermine the union.[\\[426\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllis2004202Twohig2001-440)\
\
During the [Revolutionary War](/wiki/American_Revolutionary_War "American Revolutionary War"), Washington's views on slavery began to change.[\\[398\\]](#cite_note-Maloy_(April_6,_2021)-412) In a 1778 letter to [Lund Washington](/wiki/Lund_Washington "Lund Washington"), he made clear his desire "to get quit of Negroes" when discussing the exchange of slaves for the land he wanted to buy.[\\[427\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan2005416\'96417-441) The next year, Washington stated his intention not to separate enslaved families as a result of "a change of masters".[\\[428\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan2005417-442) During the 1780s, Washington privately expressed his support for gradual emancipation.[\\[429\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllis2004201-443) In the 1780s, he gave moral support to a plan proposed by Lafayette to purchase land and free slaves to work on it, but declined to participate in the experiment.[\\[411\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTETwohig2001-425)\
\
Washington privately expressed support for emancipation to prominent Methodists [Thomas Coke](/wiki/Thomas_Coke_(bishop) "Thomas Coke (bishop)") and [Francis Asbury](/wiki/Francis_Asbury "Francis Asbury") in 1785 but declined to sign their petition.[\\[430\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan2000292-444) In personal correspondence the next year, he made clear his desire to see the institution of slavery ended by a gradual legislative process, a view that correlated with the mainstream antislavery literature published in the 1780s that Washington possessed.[\\[431\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan2005418\'96419Furstenberg2011273\'96274,_284\'96285-445) He significantly reduced his purchases of slaves after the war but continued to acquire them in small numbers.[\\[432\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTETwohig2001122\'96123Morgan2005419Morgan2000289-446)\
\
In 1788, Washington declined a suggestion from a leading French abolitionist, [Jacques Brissot](/wiki/Jacques_Pierre_Brissot "Jacques Pierre Brissot"), to establish an abolitionist society in Virginia, stating that although he supported the idea, the time was not yet right.[\\[433\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFurstenberg2011280-447) Historian [Philip D. Morgan](/wiki/Philip_D._Morgan "Philip D. Morgan") wrote that Washington was determined not to risk national unity.[\\[434\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTETwohig2001126\'96127Morgan2000290,_299Ellis2004202-448) Washington never responded to any of the antislavery petitions he received, and the subject was not mentioned in either his last address to Congress or his Farewell Address.[\\[435\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTETwohig2001126\'96127Morgan2000297Ferling2009363-449)\
\
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Tobias_Lear.jpg/150px-Tobias_Lear.jpg)](/wiki/File:Tobias_Lear.jpg)\
\
In 1794, Washington privately told [Tobias Lear](/wiki/Tobias_Lear "Tobias Lear"), his personal secretary, that he found slavery repugnant\
\
The first clear indication that Washington seriously intended to free his slaves appears in a letter written to his secretary, [Tobias Lear](/wiki/Tobias_Lear "Tobias Lear"), in 1794.[\\[436\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllis2004257-450) Washington instructed Lear to find buyers for his land in western Virginia, explaining in a private coda that he was doing so "to liberate a certain species of property which I possess, very repugnantly to my own feelings".[\\[437\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWiencek2003274-451) The plan, along with others Washington considered in 1795 and 1796, could not be realized because he failed to find buyers for his land, his reluctance to break up slave families, and the refusal of the Custis heirs to help prevent such separations by freeing their dower slaves at the same time.[\\[438\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan2005423\'96424-452)\
\
On July 9, 1799, Washington finished making his last will; the longest provision concerned slavery. All his slaves were to be freed after the death of his wife. Washington said he did not free them immediately because his slaves intermarried with his wife's dower slaves. He forbade their sale or transportation out of Virginia. The provision also provided that old and young freed people be taken care of indefinitely; younger ones were to be taught to read and write and placed in suitable occupations.[\\[439\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan2005404-453) Washington emancipated 123 slaves, one of the few large slave-holding Virginians during the Revolutionary Era to do so.[\\[440\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan20051404\'96405Wiencek2003352\'9635-454)[\\[441\\]](#cite_note-455)\
\
On January 1, 1801, one year after George Washington's death, Martha Washington signed an order to free his slaves. Many of them, having never strayed far from Mount Vernon, were reluctant to leave; others refused to abandon spouses or children still held as dower slaves by the Custis estate and also stayed with or near Martha.[\\[442\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010802-456) Following Washington's instructions in his will, funds were used to feed and clothe the young, aged, and infirm slaves until the early 1830s.[\\[443\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010815-457)\
\
Historical reputation and legacy\
--------------------------------\
\
Further information: [Legacy of George Washington](/wiki/Legacy_of_George_Washington "Legacy of George Washington") and [Historical rankings of presidents of the United States](/wiki/Historical_rankings_of_presidents_of_the_United_States "Historical rankings of presidents of the United States")\
\
See also: [Cultural depictions of George Washington](/wiki/Cultural_depictions_of_George_Washington "Cultural depictions of George Washington")\
\
[![portrait of Washington seated facing left by Gilbert Stuart](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Stuart-george-washington-constable-1797.jpg/220px-Stuart-george-washington-constable-1797.jpg)](/wiki/File:Stuart-george-washington-constable-1797.jpg)\
\
_Washington, the Constable_, a 1797 portrait by [Gilbert Stuart](/wiki/Gilbert_Stuart "Gilbert Stuart")\
\
Washington's legacy endures as one of the most influential in American history since he served as commander-in-chief of the [Continental Army](/wiki/Continental_Army "Continental Army"), a hero of the [Revolution](/wiki/American_Revolution "American Revolution"), and the first [president of the United States](/wiki/President_of_the_United_States "President of the United States"). Various historians maintain that he also was a dominant factor in America's founding.[\\[444\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling2009xviii-458) [Revolutionary War](/wiki/American_Revolutionary_War "American Revolutionary War") comrade [Henry Lee](/wiki/Henry_Lee_III "Henry Lee III") [eulogized him](https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/The_Father_of_His_Country "s:The Father of His Country") as "First in war, first in peace, and first in the hearts of his countrymen".[\\[445\\]](#cite_note-459) Lee's words became the hallmark by which Washington's reputation was impressed upon the American memory, with some biographers regarding him as the great exemplar of republicanism. He set many precedents for the national government and the presidency in particular, and he was called the "Father of His Country" as early as 1778.[\\[o\\]](#cite_note-461)[\\[447\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUnger2013236\'96237ParryAllison1991xiHindle201792-462)[\\[448\\]](#cite_note-463)\
\
Washington became an international symbol for liberation and nationalism as the leader of the first successful revolution against a colonial empire. The [Federalists](/wiki/Federalist_Party "Federalist Party") made him the symbol of their party, but the [Jeffersonians](/wiki/Democratic-Republican_Party "Democratic-Republican Party") continued to distrust his influence for years and delayed building the [Washington Monument](/wiki/Washington_Monument "Washington Monument").[\\[449\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTECunliffe195824\'9626-464) Washington was elected a member of the [American Academy of Arts and Sciences](/wiki/American_Academy_of_Arts_and_Sciences "American Academy of Arts and Sciences") on January 31, 1781.[\\[450\\]](#cite_note-465)\
\
In 1879, Congress proclaimed [Washington's Birthday](/wiki/Presidents%27_Day "Presidents' Day") to be a federal holiday.[\\[451\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling20094-466) Through a congressional joint resolution [Public Law 94-479](https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Public_Law_94-479 "s:Public Law 94-479"), passed on January 19, 1976, with an effective appointment date of July 4, 1976, he was posthumously appointed to the grade of General of the Armies of the United States during the [American Bicentennial](/wiki/United_States_Bicentennial "United States Bicentennial"). President [Gerald Ford](/wiki/Gerald_Ford "Gerald Ford") stated that Washington would "rank first among all officers of the Army, past and present".[\\[p\\]](#cite_note-468)[\\[453\\]](#cite_note-469) On March 13, 1978, Washington was militarily promoted to the rank of [General of the Armies](/wiki/General_of_the_Armies "General of the Armies").[\\[454\\]](#cite_note-Army_1978-470)\
\
In 1809, [Mason Locke Weems](/wiki/Mason_Locke_Weems "Mason Locke Weems") wrote a [hagiographic](/wiki/Hagiography "Hagiography") biography to honor Washington.[\\[455\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeems191822-471) Historian Ron Chernow maintains that Weems attempted to humanize Washington, making him look less stern, and to inspire "patriotism and morality" and to foster "enduring myths", such as Washington's refusal to lie about damaging his father's cherry tree.[\\[456\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010813\'96814Levy20136,_217Weems191822-472)[\\[457\\]](#cite_note-473) Weems' accounts have never been proven or disproven.[\\[458\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTELevy20136-474) Historian John Ferling, however, maintains that Washington remains the only founder and president ever to be referred to as "godlike", and points out that his character has been the most scrutinized by historians.[\\[459\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling2009xviii\'96xix-475) Biographer [Douglas Southall Freeman](/wiki/Douglas_Southall_Freeman "Douglas Southall Freeman") concluded, "The great big thing stamped across that man is character." Expanding on Freeman's assessment, historian [David Hackett Fischer](/wiki/David_Hackett_Fischer "David Hackett Fischer") defined Washington's character as "integrity, self-discipline, courage, absolute honesty, resolve, and decision, but also forbearance, decency, and respect for others".[\\[460\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFischer2004446-476)\
\
In the 21st century, Washington's reputation has been critically scrutinized.\
\
[Ron Chernow](/wiki/Ron_Chernow "Ron Chernow") describes Washington as always trying to be even-handed in dealing with the Natives. He states that Washington hoped they would abandon their itinerant hunting life and adapt to fixed agricultural communities in the manner of white settlers. He also maintains that Washington never advocated outright confiscation of tribal land or the forcible removal of tribes and that he berated American settlers who abused natives, admitting that he held out no hope for peaceful relations as long as "frontier settlers entertain the opinion that there is not the same crime (or indeed no crime at all) in killing a native as in killing a white man."[\\[461\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010666-477)\
\
By contrast, [Colin G. Calloway](/wiki/Colin_G._Calloway "Colin G. Calloway") wrote that, "Washington had a lifelong obsession with getting Indian land, either for himself or for his nation, and initiated policies and campaigns that had devastating effects in Indian country."[\\[462\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalloway201838-478) He stated:\
\
> The growth of the nation demanded the dispossession of Indian people. Washington hoped the process could be bloodless and that Indian people would give up their lands for a "fair" price and move away. But if Indians refused and resisted, as they often did, he felt he had no choice but to "extirpate" them and that the expeditions he sent to destroy Indian towns were therefore entirely justified.[\\[463\\]](#cite_note-479)\
\
Along with other Founding Fathers, Washington has been condemned for holding enslaved people. Though he expressed the desire to see the abolition of slavery come through legislation, he did not initiate or support any initiatives for bringing about its end. This has led to calls from some activists to remove his name from public buildings and his statue from public spaces.[\\[464\\]](#cite_note-480) Nonetheless, Washington maintains his place among the highest-ranked U.S. Presidents.[\\[465\\]](#cite_note-481)\
\
### Places, namesakes, and monuments\
\
Further information: [List of memorials to George Washington](/wiki/List_of_memorials_to_George_Washington "List of memorials to George Washington") and [List of statues of George Washington](/wiki/List_of_statues_of_George_Washington "List of statues of George Washington")\
\
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Washington_Monument_Dusk_Jan_2006.jpg/95px-Washington_Monument_Dusk_Jan_2006.jpg)](/wiki/File:Washington_Monument_Dusk_Jan_2006.jpg)\
\
The [Washington Monument](/wiki/Washington_Monument "Washington Monument")\
\
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Mount_Rushmore_distant_view.jpg/219px-Mount_Rushmore_distant_view.jpg)](/wiki/File:Mount_Rushmore_distant_view.jpg)\
\
_Shrine of Democracy_, [Mount Rushmore](/wiki/Mount_Rushmore "Mount Rushmore") National Memorial\
\
Many places and monuments have been named in honor of Washington, most notably [Washington, D.C.](/wiki/Washington,_D.C. "Washington, D.C."), the capital of the United States, and the state of [Washington](/wiki/Washington_(state) "Washington (state)"), the only U.S. state to be named after a president.[\\[466\\]](#cite_note-482)\
\
On February 21, 1885, the [Washington Monument](/wiki/Washington_Monument "Washington Monument") was dedicated. The 555-foot marble [obelisk](/wiki/Obelisk "Obelisk"), which stands on the [National Mall](/wiki/National_Mall "National Mall") in Washington, D.C., was built between 1848\'961854 and 1879\'961884 and was the tallest structure in the world between 1884 and 1889.[\\[467\\]](#cite_note-483)\
\
Washington appears as one of four U.S. presidents on the _Shrine of Democracy_, a colossal statue by [Gutzon Borglum](/wiki/Gutzon_Borglum "Gutzon Borglum") on [Mount Rushmore](/wiki/Mount_Rushmore "Mount Rushmore") in [South Dakota](/wiki/South_Dakota "South Dakota").[\\[468\\]](#cite_note-484)\
\
A number of [secondary schools and universities](/wiki/Washington_(disambiguation)#Education "Washington (disambiguation)") are named in honor of Washington, including [George Washington University](/wiki/George_Washington_University "George Washington University") and [Washington University in St. Louis](/wiki/Washington_University_in_St._Louis "Washington University in St. Louis").[\\[469\\]](#cite_note-485)[\\[470\\]](#cite_note-486)\
\
### Currency and postage\
\
Further information: [Presidents of the United States on U.S. postage stamps](/wiki/Presidents_of_the_United_States_on_U.S._postage_stamps "Presidents of the United States on U.S. postage stamps")\
\
Washington appears on contemporary U.S. currency, including the [one-dollar bill](/wiki/United_States_one-dollar_bill "United States one-dollar bill"), the [Presidential one-dollar coin](/wiki/Presidential_dollar_coins "Presidential dollar coins") and the [quarter-dollar coin](/wiki/Quarter_(United_States_coin) "Quarter (United States coin)") (the [Washington quarter](/wiki/Washington_quarter "Washington quarter")). Washington and [Benjamin Franklin](/wiki/Benjamin_Franklin "Benjamin Franklin") appeared on the [nation's first postage stamps](/wiki/File:First_US_Postage_stamps_of_1847.tiff "File:First US Postage stamps of 1847.tiff") in 1847. Washington has since appeared on many postage issues, more than any other person.[\\[471\\]](#cite_note-487)\
\
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Washington_1862_Issue-24c.jpg/97px-Washington_1862_Issue-24c.jpg)](/wiki/File:Washington_1862_Issue-24c.jpg)\
\
Washington issue of 1862\
\
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Washington_WF_1917_Issue-5c_%28cropped%29.jpg/103px-Washington_WF_1917_Issue-5c_%28cropped%29.jpg)](/wiki/File:Washington_WF_1917_Issue-5c_(cropped).jpg)\
\
[Washington\'96Franklin](/wiki/Washington%E2%80%93Franklin_Issues "Washington\'96Franklin Issues") issue of 1917\
\
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/2006_Quarter_Proof.png/122px-2006_Quarter_Proof.png)](/wiki/File:2006_Quarter_Proof.png)\
\
Washington [quarter dollar](/wiki/Quarter_(United_States_coin) "Quarter (United States coin)")\
\
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/George_Washington_Presidential_%241_Coin_obverse.png/120px-George_Washington_Presidential_%241_Coin_obverse.png)](/wiki/File:George_Washington_Presidential_$1_Coin_obverse.png)\
\
Washington [Presidential one-dollar coin](/wiki/Presidential_dollar_coins "Presidential dollar coins")\
\
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/US_one_dollar_bill%2C_obverse%2C_series_2009.jpg/280px-US_one_dollar_bill%2C_obverse%2C_series_2009.jpg)](/wiki/File:US_one_dollar_bill,_obverse,_series_2009.jpg)\
\
Washington on the 2009 [dollar bill](/wiki/United_States_one-dollar_bill "United States one-dollar bill")\
\

Notes\
-----\
\
.mw-parser-output .reflist\{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em;list-style-type:decimal\}.mw-parser-output .reflist .references\{font-size:100%;margin-bottom:0;list-style-type:inherit\}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2\{column-width:30em\}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3\{column-width:25em\}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns\{margin-top:0.3em\}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns ol\{margin-top:0\}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns li\{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column\}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-alpha\{list-style-type:upper-alpha\}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-roman\{list-style-type:upper-roman\}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-alpha\{list-style-type:lower-alpha\}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-greek\{list-style-type:lower-greek\}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-roman\{list-style-type:lower-roman\}\
\
1.  **[^](#cite_ref-3)** Contemporaneous records used the [Old Style](/wiki/Old_Style_and_New_Style_dates "Old Style and New Style dates") Julian calendar and the [Annunciation Style](/wiki/New_Year#Historical_European_new_year_dates "New Year") of enumerating years, recording his birth as February 11, 1731. The British [Calendar (New Style) Act 1750](/wiki/Calendar_(New_Style)_Act_1750 "Calendar (New Style) Act 1750") implemented in 1752 altered the official British dating method to the Gregorian calendar with the start of the year on January\'a01 (it had been March 25). These changes resulted in dates being moved forward 11 days and an advance of one year for those between January\'a01 and March 25. For a further explanation, see [Old Style and New Style dates](/wiki/Old_Style_and_New_Style_dates "Old Style and New Style dates").[\\[2\\]](#cite_note-2)\
2.  **[^](#cite_ref-13)** Washington received his license through the college, whose charter gave it the authority to appoint Virginia county surveyors. There is no evidence that he actually attended classes there.[\\[11\\]](#cite_note-professional-surveys-12)\
3.  **[^](#cite_ref-26)** The mid-16th-century word "Indian" described the Indigenous Peoples of the Americas. More modern terms for Indians include American Indian and Native American and Indigenous Peoples.[\\[23\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTECresswell2010222-25)\
4.  **[^](#cite_ref-43)** A second Virginia regiment was raised under Colonel William Byrd III and also allocated to the expedition.[\\[39\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlexner1965206\'96207-42)\
5.  **[^](#cite_ref-54)** Some descendants of [West Ford](/wiki/West_Ford_(slave) "West Ford (slave)"), a slave of [John Augustine Washington](/wiki/John_Augustine_Washington "John Augustine Washington")'s, maintain (based on family [oral history](/wiki/Oral_history "Oral history")) that Ford was fathered by George Washington, though historians dispute his paternity.[\\[48\\]](#cite_note-52)[\\[49\\]](#cite_note-53)\
6.  **[^](#cite_ref-76)** In a letter of September 20, 1765, Washington protested to "Robert Cary & Co." that the low prices he received for his tobacco and for the inflated prices he was forced to pay on second-rate goods from London.[\\[70\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010138Ferling200968-75)\
7.  **[^](#cite_ref-110)** Congress initially directed the war effort in June 1776 with the committee known as "Board of War and Ordnance"; this was succeeded by the [Board of War](/wiki/Board_of_War "Board of War") in July 1777, which eventually included members of the military.[\\[103\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFreedman200842-109)\
8.  **[^](#cite_ref-205)** Thomas Jefferson praised Washington for his "moderation and virtue" in relinquishing command. Reportedly, upon being informed of Washington's plans by painter [Benjamin West](/wiki/Benjamin_West "Benjamin West"), King George III remarked: "If he does that, he will be the greatest man in the world."[\\[197\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010454Taylor2016319\'96320-204)\
9.  **[^](#cite_ref-208)** The Society of the Cincinnati was formed by Henry Knox in May 1783, to carry on the memory of the War of Independence and to establish a fraternity of officers. The Society was named after [Cincinnatus](/wiki/Lucius_Quintus_Cincinnatus "Lucius Quintus Cincinnatus"), a famous Roman military leader who relinquished his position after his Roman victory at [Algidus (458 BC)](/wiki/Battle_of_Mount_Algidus "Battle of Mount Algidus"). However, he had reservations about some of the society's precepts, including heredity requirements for membership and receiving money from foreign interests.[\\[199\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010444,_461,_498Ferling2009xxParsons189896Brumwell2012412-207)\
10.  **[^](#cite_ref-231)** Starting in 1774, 14 men served as [President of the Continental Congress](/wiki/President_of_the_Continental_Congress "President of the Continental Congress") but bore no relationship to the presidency established under Article II of the Constitution. Under the Articles of Confederation, Congress called its presiding officer "President of the United States in Congress Assembled", but this position had no national executive powers.[\\[221\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJensen1948178\'96179Unger201361,_146JillsonWilson199477-230)\
11.  **[^](#cite_ref-238)** There has been debate over whether Washington added "so help me God" to the end of the oath.[\\[227\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIrving1857475Alden1996236-237)\
12.  **[^](#cite_ref-363)** The first account of Washington's death was written by doctors Craik and Brown, published in _The Times_ of Alexandria five days after his death on December 19, 1799. The complete text can be found in _The Eclectic Medical Journal_ (1858).[\\[351\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTENewtonFreemanBickley1858273\'96274-362)\
13.  **[^](#cite_ref-368)** Modern medical experts who blamed medical malpractice include Morens and Wallenborn in 1999,[\\[352\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWallenborn1999Morens19991845\'961849-364)[\\[353\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010809-365) Cheatham in 2008,[\\[354\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTECheatham2008-366) and Vadakan in 2005.[\\[355\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVadakan2005-367)\
14.  **[^](#cite_ref-396)** The Constitution came under attack in Pennsylvania, and Washington wrote to Richard Peters, "It would seem from the public Gazettes that the minority in your State are preparing for another attack of the now adopted Government; how formidable it may be, I know not. But that Providence which has hitherto smiled on the honest endeavours of the well meaning part of the People of this Country will not, I trust, withdraw its support from them at this crisis."[\\[382\\]](#cite_note-395)\
15.  **[^](#cite_ref-461)** The earliest known _image_ in which Washington is identified as the Father of His Country is in the frontispiece of a 1779 German-language almanac, with calculations by [David Rittenhouse](/wiki/David_Rittenhouse "David Rittenhouse") and published by Francis Bailey in Lancaster County Pennsylvania. _Der Gantz Neue Verbesserte Nord-Americanische Calendar_ has a personification of Fame holding a trumpet to her lips juxtaposed with an image of Washington and the words "_Der Landes Vater_" ("the father of the country" or "the father of the land").[\\[446\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTELightnerReeder1953133-460)\
16.  **[^](#cite_ref-468)** In _Portraits & Biographical Sketches of the United States Army's Senior Officer_, William Gardner Bell states that Washington was recalled to military service from his retirement in 1798, and "Congress passed legislation that would have made him General of the Armies of the United States, but his services were not required in the field, and the appointment was not made until the Bicentennial in 1976 when it was bestowed posthumously as a commemorative honor."[\\[452\\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBell199252,_66-467)\
\

56.  **[^](#cite_ref-61)** Bish, Jim (Spring 2010). ["Hugh West and the West Family's Momentous Role in Founding and Developing Alexandria and Fairfax and Loudoun Counties, Virginia"](https://alexandriahistorical.org/wp-content/uploads/2018/05/2010_Sp_Chronicle.pdf) (PDF). _The Alexandria Chronicle_. Alexandria Historical Society. pp.\'a013\'9614. [Archived](https://web.archive.org/web/20210423171740/https://alexandriahistorical.org/wp-content/uploads/2018/05/2010_Sp_Chronicle.pdf) (PDF) from the original on April 23, 2021. Retrieved May 10, 2020. In 1755 Hugh West Jr. gave up his seat in Fairfax County and won a House of Burgess election in Frederick County defeating Colonel George Washington. This defeat was Washington's only electoral loss. Hugh West Jr. served as a Frederick County burgess until 1758 when he was defeated by Washington.\
57.  **[^](#cite_ref-62)** ["To George Washington from Adam Stephen, 23 December 1755"](https://founders.archives.gov/documents/Washington/02-02-02-0229). National Historical Publications and Records Commission (The U.S. National Archives and Records Administration). 1755. Retrieved May 10, 2020. GW kept a copy of the Frederick County poll sheet (c.\'a010 Dec., DLC:GW) in his papers with the names of the 40 men who voted for him and the names of the 271 men who voted for Hugh West and 270 who voted for Thomas Swearingen.\

466.  **[^](#cite_ref-482)** ["George Washington"](https://www.britannica.com/biography/George-Washington). _Britannica.com_. [Encyclop\'e6dia Britannica, Inc.](/wiki/Encyclop%C3%A6dia_Britannica,_Inc. "Encyclop\'e6dia Britannica, Inc.") July 4, 2023. His fellow citizens memorialized him forever by naming the newly created capital city of the young nation for him while he was still alive. Later, one of the states of union would bear his name\'97the only state named for an individual American. Moreover, counties in 32 states were given his name, and in time it also could be found in 121 postal addresses.\
